Recent progress in electrocatalysts and electrodes for portable fuel cells

tefan Neau a, Florentina Neau a, Iuliana M. Chirica a, Irina Borbth b, Emlia Tlas b, Andrs Tompos b, Simona Somacescu c, Petre Osiceanu c, M. Antonia Folgado d, Antonio M. Chaparro d and Mihaela Florea *a
aNational Institute of Materials Physics, 405A Atomistilor Street, 077125 Magurele, Romania. E-mail: mihaela.florea@infim.ro
bResearch Centre for Natural Sciences, Institute of Materials and Environmental Chemistry, Etvs Lornd Research Network (ELKH), Magyar Tudsok Krtja 2, H-1117 Budapest, Hungary
cIlie Murgulescu Institute of Physical Chemistry, Romanian Academy, Spl. Independentei 202, 060021 Bucharest, Romania
dDepartment of Energy, Centro de Investigaciones Energticas, Medioambientales y Tecnolgicas (CIEMAT), Avda. Complutense 40, 28040 Madrid, Spain

Received 30th April 2021 , Accepted 24th June 2021

First published on 24th June 2021

Abstract

Fuel cells are devices that transform efficiently the chemical energy of hydrogen or another fuel into clean electricity. The fuel cell technology is attractive for its high-energy efficiency and expanded fuel flexibility and it became very relevant in the last decade. Moreover, the utilization of fuel cells for portable electronic devices has seen remarkable increase in the last few years. Performances of fuel cells, among others, strongly depend on the types of electrocatalysts and membrane, anion exchange or cation exchange, used in the system. Therefore, a status report about the latest advances in electrocatalysts and electrodes for portable fuel cells is the objective of this review paper. Herein, the recent progress in designing electrocatalysts for producing high performance fuel cells with truly potential applicability to be used in portable devices is highlighted.

1. Introduction

Currently, mankind is facing enormous energy and environmental problems that require urgent adoption of new energy supply technologies that utilize renewable energy sources. In this aspect, fuel cells are considered as one of the most promising efficient power generation technologies for a sustainable future.1 Such fuel cells, depending on the nature of the working fuel, can use either gaseous hydrogen or different liquids like alcohols (e.g. methanol, ethanol, propanol, ethylene glycol and glycerol among others) or non-alcohols (formic acid, dimethyl ether, hydrazine, ammonia-borane and sodium borohydride), each type possessing its own advantages and disadvantages.2

Nevertheless, the technical targets for portable power sources set by the US Department of Energy, like specific power of 4550 W kg1, peak power density (PPD) of 5570 W L1 and a cost of 75 $ per W,3 are hard to achieve using current hydrogen and direct alcohol fuel cells (DAFCs) and thus have not made sufficient progress in market presence yet. However, in terms of market implementation, among all the DAFCs, direct methanol fuel cells (DMFCs) are the most developed type, while the other types are placed in an advanced stage of research phase. For instance, in order to replace the state-of-the-art lithium ion batteries, Toshiba has invented the first prototype of a small DMFC system for a laptop computer, which can provide an average power output of 12 W with a cartridge weighing ca. 1 kg and operate continuously for at least 5 h.4

Therefore, fuel cell technologies may provide new solutions to portable power applications, to overcome current limitations in their autonomy and safety. Using hydrogen or alcohol as fuels, proton exchange membrane fuel cells (PEMFCs) have the appropriate characteristics for these objectives, like high power density, low working temperature, short start-up and shut-down times, and high efficiency.

As a difference from batteries, fuel cells exchange matter and heat with the surroundings, which determines their operation characteristics (see Fig. 1). The power response is largely limited by mass transport rates and depends on ambient conditions (temperature, humidity, orientation, natural convection) especially for air-breathing cathodes of PEMFCs. The design of the electrodes may palliate somewhat a slow start-up or large influence of the ambient environment, but, in general, fuel cells are less appropriate than batteries for some portable applications, like small and very low power devices, or for operation inside compact and closed environments. On the other hand, the same characteristic provides advantages to fuel cells with respect to batteries. There is a spatial separation between the energy storage and the power production which favours more flexible designs with no leakage currents, and makes them more suitable when requiring large autonomy, safety, or long-term energy storage. As a result, fuel cell systems will be increasingly advantageous when power and energy requirements of the portable applications increase above a few tens of W and W h. In some cases, hybrid systems comprising batteries (or capacitors) and fuel cells will be optimal, combining properties of both portable power types.


Fig. 1 Schemes showing basic working differences among a battery (a), a hydrogen fuel cell (b), and a direct alcohol fuel cell (c). Partially reproduced with permission from ref. 5, Copyright 2018 Elsevier Publications.

During the last 10 years, numerous companies developed hydrogen PEMFC and DAFC portable devices suitable to power military equipment in remote areas,6 power supplies for telecommunication base stations,7 backup power systems for an uninterruptible emergency power supply for security purposes, such as a police radio station and fire alarm system,8 power supplies to charge the equipment in an ambulance9 or even the entire vehicle,10 on-board battery charging of material handling vehicles,11 among others. Thus, the global fuel cell market size increases over time and reached an estimated value of ca. 10.48 billion $ in 2019, registering a compound annual growth rate (CAGR) of ca. 15.5% until 2027.12 In this perspective, the prospect of fuel cells for portable applications is bright and more research is needed to advance the knowledge in this field.

This work provides a thorough revision of the most promising electrocatalytic materials for portable PEMFCs so far, including hydrogen (Section 2) and direct alcohol fuel cells (DAFCs) using methanol (Section 3), ethanol (Section 4), and glycerol (Section 5). It will also provide a vision of new electrode and cell structures that should be used to improve the portability of these fuel cells (Section 6).

It should be noted that other fuels were also considered as alternatives for direct fuel cells, such as direct formic acid fuel cells (DFAFCs), direct borohydride fuel cells (DBFCs) and direct formate fuel cells (DFFCs) as power supplies in portable applications, mainly due to their easy storage and transportation and to their high theoretical open circuit voltage.1315 However, despite the huge potential of DFFC cells, they suffer from poor power output and very low durability, mainly due to the low kinetics of formic acid oxidation at low temperatures and formation of CO as a reaction product.1618 For example, a peak power density of 550 mW cm2 at 30 C was obtained using a bifunctional PdNi electrocatalyst, but with 16% loss of performance in only 4 h.19

DBFCs manage to avoid problems related to CO poisoning or poor oxidation kinetics, and exhibit performances about 1.3 times higher than PEMFCs or DMFCs. However, boron resources are limited and located mainly in Turkey, which makes it difficult to exploit them on a global scale.20 The use of formate as an alternative in DLFCs for practical applications is also in progress, and research is underway to overcome the issue of the efficiency of DFFCs that occurs after KOH removal.21

Thus, although constant efforts are made in order to increase the performance and the stability of these alternative fuel cells,2224 the scope of the current review is to cover only the direct alcohol fuel cells in the category of direct liquid fuel cells.

2. Portable hydrogenair fuel cells

2.1 Short overview

Portable applications of polymer electrolyte fuel cells are mainly centered on two large markets: the first one dedicated to portable power generators designed for light outdoor personal purposes (e.g. camping and climbing), while the second huge market is addressed to the consumer electronic devices, basically any electronic device that traditionally uses a battery such as laptops, cell phones, etc.

Powerful advantages such as the modularity and the high energy density of PEMFCs make them the most attractive candidates for future portable personal electronics. One can foresee applications as a support for any electronic device that can benefit from the DC power supply: battery charges, toys, kits in modern educational systems, gadgets, etc. Nevertheless, we have to emphasize that much progress should be achieved to strongly improve the performances in many fields such as electrocatalysis, costs, material synthesis routes, mass, heat transfer phenomena, etc.

Lithium batteries offer a power density of 200 W h kg1. H2 fuel cells, in order to be competitive in the portable application sector, are expected to generate a higher power density due to the high reaction rate at the electrodes and proton transfer rate across the PEM. In PEM, with Nafion-type membranes, the protons generated in the anode part pass into the cathode part through the membrane and the reactants and reaction products are very well separated in the cathode chamber and anode chamber respectively. This feature imposes restrictions on the operating temperature of these fuel cells. Thus, they cannot operate at temperatures above 80 C (preferably 6080 C) at atmospheric pressure. On the other hand, it should be stressed that proper operation involves the use of hydrated fuels to avoid poisoning anodes with CO. Hydrogen fuels are generally obtained from the reformation of hydrocarbons (petrol and natural gas) and most often contain 1% CO which at low temperatures favours carbon deposition. Using a working temperature of 100 C could be a solution and thus there is no need to hydrate the fuel at the anode. The development of an alternative PEM with high performance and good CO tolerance using low hydration conditions is still a challenge for the scientific community.

In many aspects the PEMFCs offer huge advantages for portable device applications, such as efficiency with high energy capacity, lightweight and straightforward construction, low cost, long life, etc.25,26 Indeed, power generation sources typically get the fuel directly through a small or light tank showing versatility and high potential towards miniaturization, an important criterion for home appliance applications.27,28 Obviously, applying fuel cells to replace conventional generators based on fossil fuels will reduce harmful atmospheric emissions and create cleaner environments with less noise pollution.

In the market of electronic devices, PEMFCs are appropriate for supplying the energy resources for individual gadgets such as netbooks, game consoles, cameras, television stations, educational packs, handheld chargers, and so on.29,30

A recent review on the portability of electricity summarized that hydrogen fuel cells (FCs) offer two advantages: (i) they provide new power generation properties, which are beneficial for those applications requiring high power and autonomy (stored energy), (ii) they can make renewable energies more accessible to portable applications of any type, with the highest efficiency and safety.5 One of the key issues of the research in this field is what new types of electrocatalysts can replace the commercial carbon black (CB) supported Pt ones in these systems on both anode and cathode side and what kind of properties the new materials can provide. Because the price of Pt accounts for a significant part of the cost of hydrogen-fed devices, it is very important to reduce the Pt content and to decrease the corrosion of the active metal. In order to achieve these goals, the development of the carbonaceous support seems to be a useful tool in parallel with the development of Pt-free electrocatalysts. The recent advances in these two aspects are presented further.

2.2 Mono- and bimetallic systems supported on new types of carbonaceous materials

New types of carbonaceous materials such as graphene (G) and its derivatives,31 especially reduced graphene oxide (r-GO) frequently obtained from graphite oxide (GO),32 furthermore carbon nanotubes (CNTs),31,33 ordered mesoporous carbon,33 and relatively cheap graphene nanoplatelets (GNPs) have been regarded as promising new electrocatalyst supports for noble metals because of their favourable electronic properties, mechanical- and chemical stability and excellent corrosion resistance. Another promising approach is the utilization of Pt-containing bimetallic supported catalysts. Several examples of new types of electrocatalysts consisting of various carbonaceous materials and active metals for hydrogen FCs are collected in Table 1.

The following brief overview of the latest works presented in Table 1 focuses mainly on the preparation of supported metal nanoparticles and the peculiarities of the use of new types of carbonaceous materials used in PEMFCs.

In an early study on graphene materials, Pt nanoparticles (NPs) were deposited onto graphene sheets by means of borohydride reduction of H2PtCl6 in a graphene oxide (GO) suspension. Furthermore, GOPt film prepared for MEA was partially reduced with hydrazine. The latter was heat treated at 300 C. Although about 80% enhancement in the electrochemically active surface area (ECSA) was achieved in the case of the reduced GOPt film after heat treatment, the fuel cell experiments showed a significant drop in power output which was explained by the loss of proton conductivity property of the Nafion ionomer (used during the deposition of the electrocatalyst) due to its changes at higher temperature.34

Other preparation methods, for instance ethylene glycol (EG) reflux, sodium borohydride reduction and ascorbic acid reduction of H2PtCl6 for the preparation of supported Pt NPs were compared and the most promising results in terms of Pt dispersion, particle size, and electrocatalytic activity were obtained by use of the EG reflux.35 The authors found that the pH of the synthesis medium and the reflux environment greatly influenced the particle size, distribution, and related parameters of the supported Pt nanoparticles during EG reduction, which significantly influenced the cell performances.36 It is worth mentioning that the alkaline pH of the H2PtCl6 precursor solution was favourable.36,37

EG reduction method was found appropriate for the synthesis of Pt NPs both onto graphene derivatives35,3840etc., pristine36 and different types of functionalized carbon nanotubes.41 Functional groups on the surface of the carbonaceous material play an important role in the anchoring of the metal NPs, that's why GO is successfully used in the preparation of metal NPs supported by graphene derivatives; furthermore, functionalization of carbon nanotubes and other carbonaceous materials is a key step.42

The microwave-assisted version of EG reduction was an effective synthesis method for the fabrication of supported Pt NPs.37,49,52 Comparison of the samples obtained by conventional heating (Pt/CNTs-CH), microwave heating (Pt/CNTs-MWH) and using commercial Pt/C (Pt/C) revealed that the microwave assisted Pt deposition resulted in the best fuel cell performance (see Table 1). According to the TEM measurements, smaller Pt nanoparticles appeared with a uniform distribution by the microwave assisted method which contributed to better performance (see Fig. 2).52


Fig. 2 HRTEM images of (a) Pt/CNTs-CH and (b) Pt/CNTs-MWH catalysts. Reproduced with permission from ref. 52, Copyright 2017 Elsevier Publications.

Successful one-pot synthesis of Pt electrocatalysts supported on novel hybrid structures with varying contents of GO and CB was also reported recently.35 Firstly, GO and CB were mixed homogeneously and then the Pt was loaded onto the hybrid support according to the polyol method by use of H2PtCl6 and EG. The one-pot synthesis represents a step forward for scaling up (see Fig. 3).35


Fig. 3 Schematic of grapheneCB hybrid support synthesis and structure. Reproduced with permission from ref. 35, Copyright 2017 Elsevier Publications.

In addition to the H2PtCl6 precursor, Pt NPs have been obtained from 1,5-dimethyl platinum cyclooctadiene (CODPtMe2) by use of the supercritical carbon dioxide deposition (scCO2) technique,44,46 and from tris(dibenzylideneacetone)platinum (Pt(dba)3) by use of an efficient and mild organometallic synthesis.47 Ultra-small Pt NPs (1.2 nm) were prepared from platinum(II) acetylacetonate in the presence of oleylamine and 1-octadecene by use of morpholine borane complex.45 Physical vapour deposition was used to form Pt NPs on a new type of graphene nanosheetcarbon nanotube (GNSCNT) hybrid material which was synthesized by direct growth.50

Regarding the bimetallic catalysts, various methods for their preparation are described in the hydrogen-FC literature. Graphene-supported Pt3M (M = Co and Cr) alloy nanoparticles were prepared by the EG reduction method.54 Pt3M alloy cathodes exhibited better performance than Pt under fuel cell conditions, as depicted in Table 1. The high catalytic performance of Pt3M alloys was ascribed to the inhibition of the formation of (hydr)oxy species on the Pt surface by the alloying elements.

GNPs supported PtNi, PtFe, and PtCu bimetallic catalysts were prepared by the supercritical carbon dioxide (scCO2) technique by use of the Pt(COD)Me2 organometallic precursor for the primary metal and nickel(II) hexafluoroacetylacetonate hydrate [Ni(hfac)2H2O], copper(II) hexafluoroacetylacetonate hydrate [Cu(hfa)2H2O] and iron(III) tris(2,2,6,6-tetramethyl-3,5-heptanedionate) [Fe(thd)3] precursors for the secondary metals (see Fig. 4). The best catalytic activity towards both hydrogen oxidation reaction (HOR) and oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) was shown by the PtNi/GNP catalyst. The same catalyst exhibited the highest performance in the fuel cell experiments when it was used as the anode electrode.58


Fig. 4 A schematic representation of the preparation process of the bimetallic catalysts by sequential deposition (OMA: Pt(COD)Me2, OMB: [Ni(hfac)2H2O], [Cu(hfa)2H2O] and [Fe(thd)3]). Reproduced with permission from ref. 58, Copyright 2020 Elsevier Publications.

For the preparation of PtCu/CNT electrocatalysts,56 the Cu/CNT template was prepared first in the presence of NaBH4 reducing agent and CuSO45H2O by three different methods: (i) reverse microemulsion, (ii) chemical reduction and (iii) sonochemistry. Pt was deposited according to the following equation (eqn (1)):

3Cu/CNTsup + PtCl62 PtCu/CNTsup + 2Cu2+ + 6Cl(1)

The highest mass catalytic activity was obtained in the case of the PtCu/CNTson, sample prepared by sonication.56 Multi-wall carbon nanotube (MWCNT) supported PtAu electrocatalyst was synthesized by use of the reverse microemulsion method which was scaled up to the preparation of MEA of 9 cm2.55 A microemulsion method was used in order to synthesize reduced graphene oxide (r-GO) supported Pt and PtAu nanoparticles, too.57

In addition to a variety of supported active metal particles, a similarly large variety of carbonaceous supports can be found in the recent literature on hydrogen FCs. The so-called graphene support was very often prepared from natural flake graphite by use of a strong oxidizing agent, most frequently by the modified Hummers' method,35,39,44,49,57etc. Although r-GO prepared by the reduction of functionalized graphene sheets significantly differs from the graphene from other sources in the disorder and number defective sites, this approach is beneficial for the stabilization of highly dispersed metal NPs.

A recent study compared the effectiveness of the different new types of carbonaceous materials as supports of electrocatalysts of hydrogen FCs.37 The different carbon supports did not significantly affect the Pt particle size during the synthesis, but led to different amounts of defective sites in the carbon framework so the availability of active metal sites and metalsupport interactions were influenced. MWCNTs were found to be the most suitable carbon support for Pt catalysts because the Pt/MWCNT sample exhibited high activity and stability as a cathode in PEM fuel cells due to its Pt utilization efficiency and lower overpotential loss.37 In another study, the value of kinetic density current at 0.7 V was higher in PtAu/MWCNTs than in the Pt/C commercial material. It was concluded that MWCNTs as the support provided improved electronic charge transference compared to the traditional carbonaceous materials.55

The effectiveness of MWCNTs as an electrocatalyst support was enhanced by the wrapping of graphene around it. Carbon nanotubes in the above composite were well-separated and the highly dispersed MWCNTs through anchoring sites contributed to the homogeneous deposition of ultra-nano Pt particles over the composites.53

Pt catalysts supported by the GNSCNT hybrid type of carbonaceous material exhibited superior performance in hydrogen FC tests. High catalytic utilization of Pt was explained by the uniform dispersion of nanoscopic Pt nanoparticles on the GNS which was reflected in the high ECSA values. GNS provided high surface area while CNTs decreased the mass transport limitations of the composite. Besides, CNS-CNTs were grown directly on carbon cloth providing a direct electron-transfer pathway and reducing energy losses between interfaces.50

The performance of graphene itself as a catalyst support has often been found to be less favourable than that of other carbon materials, including conventional CB,37,45 but there are some exceptions.43 The comparison may be complicated by the fact that many other parameters have to be the same for correct conclusions about the effect of the carbonaceous support, e.g. metal load, particle size distribution, metalsupport-interaction, etc. It is generally accepted that due to the 2D dense structure of the G or r-GO support, strong mass transport limitations and relatively low Pt utilization are found in this type of catalysts. However, the examples shown in Table 1 illustrate that hybrid supports created by combining different types of carbonaceous materials are able to provide better results than the parent carbonaceous material alone. Use of hybrids of G, r-GO, and GNPs47 with MWCNTs, with differently functionalized MWCNTs,41,51 and even with CB led to enhanced maximum power density in the hydrogen FC experiments. When porous Ptgraphene/MWCNT composite cathodes were fabricated from the G supported Pt catalyst and from acid treated MWCNTs the maximum power density of the composite supported Pt was four fold, although the ECSA of the Pt in the Ptgraphene/MWCNT composite cathode was only slightly larger than that for the Pt in the Ptgraphene cathode.49

Several studies have also shown that the proportion of components in hybrid carbon supports strongly influences the performance of the electrocatalysts.35,38,41,51 As an example, the combination of 2D acid functionalized graphene (f-G) and 1D acid functionalized multiwalled carbon nanotubes (f-MWNTs) in equal proportion as a catalyst support for 3D Pt NPs gave the best performance due to the good accessibility and uniform dispersion of the nanostructured Pt.51 Field emission scanning electron microscopy (FE-SEM) images of hybridized Pt/rGO-MWCNT samples with different mass ratios of rGO and MWCNTs41 indicated significant morphological differences (see Fig. 5) which led to a variety of electrocatalytic performances (see Table 1).


Fig. 5 FE-SEM images of hybridized Pt/rGO-MWCNTs with different mass ratios of rGO and MWCNTs. Reproduced with permission from ref. 41, Copyright 2018 Elsevier Publications.

Other ways of producing the 3D structure have also proven to be effective. For instance, when graphene was intercalated by nano-B4C ceramic, the electrochemical activity and stability of the Pt catalyst greatly improved as the Ptr-GO/B4C electrode had superior mass transfer capability for reaction species than the Ptr-GO electrode.59

The improvement effect of the spacer components was shown even when new types of carbonaceous materials were layered during the preparation of the MEA. As some examples, the hybrid cathode layer built from commercial Pt/C catalyst and r-GO supported Au NPs62 or I-doped graphene60,61 was able to increase the maximum power density.

The beneficial effect of I-doped graphene was attributed to that the chemical iodination increased the charge carrier concentration in the -band of graphene layers, favouring the obtaining of a metallic nature. It reduced the resistance in PEMFCs as well as facilitated oxygen transport toward catalytic sites without severe water flooding.61 CNTs and PtAu/CNTs were used as spacers during the preparation of MEA for FC experiments.57 Pt/rGO and PtAu/rGO catalysts without a spacer showed moderate performance as the graphene-based support did not facilitate the elimination of the water flooding and the dense structure of the GDL was not adequate for the diffusion of the gases. The introduction of the spacer improved the structure, the ohmic resistance and the metal dispersion of the GDL. As a result the maximum power density value of the PtAu/rGO increased 1.8 and 5.2 times, when CNTs and PtAu/CNT spacer were used.57

A strong argument in favour of the use of graphene and carbon nanotube-based catalyst supports is that they have been shown to be more resistant to corrosion and thus can provide durability. The addition of a small amount of the Pt/GO catalyst significantly enhanced the durability of the commercial Pt/C catalyst without sacrificing the initial ECSA.40 Pt/graphene catalyst was considered more advantageous than traditional carbon supported Pt, because GO derived graphene maintained strong adhesion with Pt particles and provided strong corrosion resistance.39 CNTs exhibited a remarkable catalytic stability up to 30000 cycles; its FC performance significantly exceeded that of the commercial Pt/C catalyst.56 However, a number of other solutions are being sought to achieve durability by modifying the carbon support. In a recent work Pt/C electrocatalyst was treated with vanadium pentoxide (V2O5) and phosphoric acid to produce Pt/C-VO(H2PO4)2 catalyst.63 This combination improved the durability of proton exchange membrane fuel cells because it reduced the oxidation of the cathode catalyst in the weak acid environment.63

2.3 Platinum-free electrocatalysts

The limited reserves of platinum in the Earth's crust motivated researchers to develop PEMFCs with platinum free electrocatalysts with better performances and stability.

An original approach related to the catalyst's design for the hydrogen oxidation reaction (HOR) was recently published by Reuillard et al.64 They developed a novel catalyst for HOR by using graphene acid (GA) nanosheets for the noncovalent immobilization of a bioinspired nickel bis-diphosphine [NiII(P2CyN2Arg)2]7+ (NiArg). The GA-modified electrodes were prepared through vacuum filtration and coated with a hydrophobic microporous layer. The current densities were above 30 mA cm2 at 0.4 V versus reversible hydrogen electrode under acidic aqueous conditions and at room temperature. This unique class of bidirectional molecular catalysts exhibits electrocatalytic activity for H2/H+ interconversion. The optimization of the catalytic layers is underway, taking into account the prevention of leaching, maximization of the number of active sites by increasing the film thickness with retention of high porosity, prevention of electrode flooding by the rationalized formulation as well as improvement of the anode stability over long time operation. The schematic representation of the functionalization process of GA is shown in Fig. 6.


Fig. 6 Schematic representations of (a) NiArg simplified chemical structure, (b) GA sheets bearing CO2H anchoring functions, and (c) GA|NiArg composite modified electrode. Reproduced with permission from ref. 64, Copyright 2020 American Chemical Society.

Allerston et al.65 prepared a PdIrAu/C ternary alloy catalyst supported on carbon by a sol-immobilisation method, involving polyvinyl alcohol (PVA) as the dispersant, sodium borohydride (NaBH4) as the reducing agent and Vulcan XC-72R as the support. The MEAs were developed by standard EU protocol tests, which include leak tests, cell break-in, conditioning, polarisation curves and load cycles (AST). They highlighted the effect of composition on the electrochemical performance in the HOR. Thus, at 0.6 V after 1000 AST cycles the PdIrAu/C catalyst exhibits performance enhancement by 36%, proved by the current density of 1100 mA cm2 compared with 700 mA cm2 obtained for PdIr/C. Also, the Au addition led to a more stable anode for the HOR, as confirmed by the results from the ICP-MS measurements.

Pu et al.66 proposed a facile and scalable synthesis strategy for precious-metal phosphides (PMPs) supported on graphene (rGO) catalysts (IrP2rGO, Rh2PrGO, RuPrGO, and Pd3PrGO) electrochemically active in the HOR in H2-saturated 0.1 M perchloric acid media using a half-cell. They obtained a remarkable HOR activity. The results indicated that the IrP2rGO performance was very close to that of the 20 wt% Pt/C commercial powder and they recommend this catalyst as an excellent candidate for the applications of water electrolysers and PEMFCs.

Recently, Park et al.67 developed a system which facilitated rapid testing and characterization of platinum group metal-free (PGM free) catalysts. As a non-platinum catalyst for the ORR they used ironcyanamidepolyanilinezinc catalyst ((CM + PANI)FeC(Zn)) from Los Alamos National Laboratory. This system was able to assess the performance of the 25 PEMFC electrodes (Fig. 7).


Fig. 7 MEA and die-cut Teflon gaskets in the array cell hardware; cathodes: 251 cm diameter discs punched from a PGM-free GDE with a catalyst loading of 4.1 mg cm2; anode: 10 cm 10 cm GDE with 0.2 mgPt cm2. Reproduced with permission from ref. 67, Copyright 2020 Elsevier Publications.

An automated deposition system assured deposition of the crack-free PGM-free catalyst electrode layer with uniform thickness, without large agglomerates, and with good adhesion to the membrane. By using this procedure the system can make robust PGM-free electrodes over the entire electrode area and the membrane does not deteriorate (see Fig. 8).


Fig. 8 (a) SEM images of the electrode surface and (b) optical microscopy cross-sectional images of a half CCM fabricated by depositing a catalystionomer ink onto a membrane using the automated ink deposition system. Reproduced with permission from ref. 67, Copyright 2020 Elsevier Publications.

The main aim of their work was to boost the scientific progress and practical implementation of PGM-free catalysts as valuable replacements for the currently used high cost platinum-based catalysts. Indeed, this is one of the main obstacles to the widespread commercialization of PEMFCs in automotive and stationary applications, accounting for 40% of the total cost of the PEMFC stack.

They studied the effect of the ink composition on the power density obtained. They used different ionomer to catalyst ratios and different isopropanol (IPA)/water ratios. The combinatorial analysis revealed that an I/C of 0.5 (33 wt%) gives the highest H2air cell performance at <0.65 V while an I/C of 1 (50 wt%) gives the highest performance at >0.65 V. On the other hand, the highest overall performance was achieved by using an IPA to water ratio of 3:7.

Nabae et al.68 prepared non-precious metal (NPM) catalysts for the ORR by the pyrolysis of spherical polyimide nanoparticles that contain small amounts of Fe additive with diameters around 60 nm. They optimized the synthesis conditions (precursor and polymerization conditions) in order to decrease the size of the polyimide particles. Thus, the synthesis protocol involved the use of a triamine monomer, 1,3,5-tris(4-aminophenyl)benzene (TAPB), instead of ODA in the presence of a dispersant as well as a carbonization process by multistep pyrolysis where the Fe species catalyses carbonization up to 600 C. They removed the excess of Fe species to minimize the loss of nitrogen species at higher temperature. They detected by XPS the nitrogen chemical species (N-pyridinic, N-pyrrolic, N-graphitic and N-oxidized) which are catalytically active sites for the ORR. The authors tested the cathode performance in a membrane electrode assembly (MEA) at 80 C using humidified hydrogen into the anode side and fully humidified oxygen or air into the cathode side. The best performance 0.62 V (O2) and 0.46 V (air) at a current density of 1 A cm2 was achieved in open circuit voltages of 0.94 V (O2) and 0.90 V (air), for the cathode with smaller particle size. Also, a new facile template method for the preparation of Ni, Co doped ZrO2 self-assembled electrocatalysts for the oxygen reduction reaction was published.69 The results indicate that the synergetic effects arising from the intimate electronic interaction of the mixed oxides present in the lattice of the zirconia phase, i.e. Ni1xCoxO (or NiO and CoO), enhance the electrocatalytic activity of the electrocatalyst.

Preda et al.70 established a versatile synthesis protocol based on a self-assembly co-templated by diethanolamine (DEA) and tripropylamine (TPA) to obtain graphene-modified NiCo2O4NiO catalysts towards the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR). The formation of the different Ni species with 3+/2+ oxidation states, proved by XPS, into a cubic spinel NiCo2O4 structure as well as the presence of the tiny amount of NiO as the secondary phase contributes to the creation of efficient active sites and enhances the conductivity. On the other hand, the electrochemical results revealed the beneficial role of the graphene addition, with an optimum amount of 10 wt%, which was able to facilitate improved mass transport, supplied more accessible active sites, and provided higher stability and better resistance to deactivation during the ORR. The synthesis route might be successfully used for large-scale applications due to its outstanding features, such as low cost, easy availability and straightforward control of the phase composition.

Banham et al.71 reported, for the first time, a pre-commercial nonprecious metal catalyst (NPMC) based MEA capable of meeting commercial performance requirements for PEMFC portable power applications. The catalyst synthesis involved the use of the polyacrylonitrile-polymethacrylic acid copolymer, 2-methylimidazole, zinc chloride (ZnCl2) as the inorganic precursor and dimethylformamide as the solvent. The raw material obtained after drying under vacuum and thermal treatment at 220 C for 3 hours was carbonized to 1100 C in a nitrogen atmosphere for 1 hour. They obtained high current densities (up to 1.5 A cm2) using a microporous catalyst >90% (nearly all pores <3 nm) and optimized the ionomer amount of the higher catalyst loading design. Thus, they achieved the highest power ever reported in air/H2 in a large-scale (50 cm2) NPMC-based MEA (570 mW cm2). Their study involved a rational catalyst layer design and thus the catalyst-level transport problems were overcome.

Chung et al.72 assessed the H2air performance of the ironnitrogencarbon catalyst with hierarchical porosity prepared using two nitrogen precursors. They achieved a maximum power density of 400 mW cm2 at 100 kPa H2air, 353 K operation temperature as well as 90 mA cm2 current density at 0.8 V.

Chandran et al.73 prepared a hybrid carbon hetero-structure based on partially oxidized carbon nanotubes (POCNTs) and PCNTs by the modified Hummers' method using multiwalled carbon nanotubes (MWNTs) synthesized by chemical vapour deposition (CVD). PCNTs possessed an accessible surface area of 125 m2 g1 measured by BET and assures good electrical conductivity. The catalyst was obtained by decoration of the support with Pd3Co alloy nanoparticles by the polyol reduction method. The performance of the MEA fuelled by H2 and O2 was assessed by using Pd3Co/PCNTs at the anode and separately at the cathode, as well as simultaneously at both the anode and cathode. The best PEMFC performance of 327 mW cm2 was achieved when the Pd3Co/PCNT catalyst was employed as the anode in a H2 atmosphere. By using Pd3Co/PCNT as cathode fed by O2, they obtained a power density of 240 mW cm2. A very important aspect of this study is related to the performance of the single cell, which is completely free from Pt, tested using a fuel cell test station. The power density of 96 mW cm2 obtained for the cell developed with both electrodes simultaneously put in light the advantage of the low cost as well as the potential applications for small portable devices.

Supported Pt-based nanoparticles are currently the state-of-the-art catalysts used for PEFC manufacturing but according to a recent analysis by the U.S. Department of Energy, about 42% of the production cost is due to platinum (Pt). Thus, platinum group metal (PGM)-free catalysts that enable the ORR in PEFCs offer a large room for cost savings and clearly represent alternative catalyst materials. Recently, Osmieri et al.74 proposed a powerful method aiming to boost both material discovery and electrode development in the field of PGM-free catalysts. The authors targeted the screening and optimization of the parameters by using a high-resolution, high throughput segmented cell (SC) accommodating more than 120 segments. Thus, the method is able to discern the high performing candidates as well as the trends in the electrode optimization process. In addition, for more accurate results the authors checked the data reproducibility for the electrodes with identical catalyst materials and fabrication methods.

Serov et al.75 recently developed a mesoporous PGM-free FeNC catalyst by using a synthesis method based on nitrogen-rich organic precursors and two types of silica sacrificial substrate. The presence of the N species such as pyridinic, hydrogenated pyrrolic and hydrogenated pyridine as well as the nitrogen coordinated to metal (Fe) was proved by XPS. The electrochemical performance of the MEA in H2/air at the temperature of the cell of 80 C with 100% RH was comparable to those of the state-of-the-art catalysts developed by the group.

2.4 Single atom electrocatalysts

Development of single atom catalysts/single atom electrocatalysts (SACs/SAECs) which is a relatively new, dynamic research area in the field of energy conversion, i.e. batteries and fuel cells, has already aroused great interest.7681 SACs combine the merits of homogeneous and heterogeneous catalysts. SACs are based on heteroatom (N, S, etc.)-doped carbon materials anchoring single metal atoms through coordination with defects, N-containing groups or unsaturated coordination sites. Since in the case of traditional catalysts obtained from platinum group metals (PGMs) the ORR occurs on the surface of the nanoparticles (NPs), any metal atoms that cannot be approached by the reactant molecules are largely a waste. SACs, due to the high density of well-defined active sites dispersed in their skeleton, exhibit not only the maximum utilization efficiency but also high catalytic activity and selectivity towards the ORR process. Depending on the type of isolated active metal atom the different categories of SACs for the ORR have been distinguished:77 (i) precious metal single atom catalysts (Pt-based-, Ir-based single atom catalysts); (ii) non-precious metal single atom catalysts (Co-based-, Fe-based-, Mn-based single atom catalysts, FeCo-, FeNi dual-metal single atom catalysts, PtCo alloy and single atom synergistic catalysts).

Several examples of SACs applied for hydrogen fuelled PEMFCs are shown in Table 2.

Different methods can be used for the synthesis of SACs; Han et al. proposed the following possibilities: (i) wet chemical methods (impregnation, modular coordination method, colloidal strategy, electrochemical deposition, low-temperature reduction method, ion exchange method); (ii) high-temperature pyrolysis methods (MOF (most frequently ZIF-8, a Zn-rich zeolitic imidazolate framework) based encapsulation pyrolysis, molten salt-assisted pyrolysis, polymer pyrolysis, metalorganic complex pyrolysis); (iii) vapour deposition method (atomic layer deposition-ALD, chemical vapour deposition-CVD); (iv) atom trapping method.78

The MNC (M: metal atom) catalysts are very often synthesized through pyrolysis, where a nitrogen containing organic precursor and metal precursor are mixed and are subsequently heated in an inert atmosphere. A general scheme for the synthesis of MNC type of SACs is given in Fig. 9.


Fig. 9 A general scheme for the synthesis of MNC type of SACs. Reproduced with permission from ref. 80, Copyright 2021 AIP Publishing.

The success or failure of the formation of SACs is often influenced by nuances, such as order of the component addition and heat treatment which led to simultaneous coordination (sample PM/ZIF-simu) or sequential coordination (sample PM/ZIF-seq) as can be seen in Fig. 10.


Fig. 10 (a) Schematic diagram of the synthesis of PM/ZIF-simu using the conventional simultaneous coordination method, which was converted to nitrogen-doped porous carbon-supported nanoparticle catalysts (NPs/NPC) after carbonization. The photo images show the non-uniform color of the PM/ZIF-simu. (b) XRD patterns of PM/ZIF-simu and pristine ZIF-8. (c) XRD patterns of the light-colored part Ir/ZIF-simu-l and dark-colored part Ir/ZIF-simu-d. (d) Schematic diagram of the synthesis of PM/ZIF-seq using the sequential coordination method, which was converted to PM1-N/C SACs after carbonization. The photo images show the uniform color of the PM/ZIF-seq. (e) XRD patterns of PM/ZIF-seq with the new phase marked by stars. Reproduced with permission from ref. 82, Copyright 2020 Wiley Online Library.

The optimal morphology resulting in high specific surface area and rich porosity of the catalysts increases the exposure of the active sites, which is important to guarantee their sufficient utilization. As an example, ZIF-8 derived nitrogen and atomic Fe dual-doped porous carbon nanocubes (Fig. 11) showed outstanding performance.


Fig. 11 Description of the preparation process of PCN-A@Fe SA nanocubes. Reproduced with permission from ref. 96, Copyright 2021 Elsevier Publications.

The porous carbon nanocubic architecture with high specific surface area and with abundant FeNx active species in the nitrogen doped carbon matrix resulted in a very effective cathode catalyst (PCN-A@Fe SA).96

Although catalysts derived from Fe-doped ZIF-8 could in theory be as active as Pt/C thanks to the high intrinsic activity of FeN4, the micropores fail to achieve rapid mass transfer.92 Therefore, Qiao et al. built an ordered hierarchical porous structure into Fe-doped ZIF-8 single crystals, which were subsequently carbonized to obtain an FeN4-doped hierarchical ordered porous carbon (FeN4/HOPC) skeleton.92 In real PEMFC tests the optimal catalyst (FeN4/HOPC-c-1000) exhibited significantly enhanced current density and power density relative to FeN4/C, which did not have an optimized pore structure.92

Non precious metal SACs are very promising candidates to replace the traditional precious-metal catalysts that are applied in the cathode of PEMFCs although many problems and challenges still remain, such as the poor stability/durability in acidic PEM surroundings, caused by the dissolution of doped Fe/Co, carbon corrosion, and protonation of the active sites.79

However, Pt SACs exhibited better stability than their traditional nanoparticulated counterparts if divided into single atoms, as demonstrated by the results of complementary techniques, including online ICP-MS, IL-TEM, and XPS.97 Nevertheless, more stable supports and dopants must be found in order to develop more robust Pt SACs.97

Recent contributions in the field using powerful tools for synthesis and analysis as well as Density Functional Theory (DFT) approach exhibit very promising results as can be demonstrated with the following examples. A cross-correlation of the experimental and theoretical results is of paramount importance and will shed more light on the SOFC field, in general and PGM-free materials, in particular. Thus recently, Holby et al.98 used DFT based approaches as a powerful theoretical tool for a comprehensive understanding of the structures at their atomic scale and their role in ORR activity as well as the active site structure stability in the PGM-free materials. The authors deeply examined the application of the aforementioned approach to an FeN4 bilayer graphene-hosted active site structure suggesting solutions to improve the active site resistance to metal dissolution in correlation with the trends in stability with respect to alkaline and acidic conditions.

A recent theoretical study published by Cao et al.,99 in line with the aforementioned publication, performs DFT calculations and ab initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulations to explore the influence of the solvation effect (as the ORR is operated in the liquid phase) on the mechanisms of the ORR on MnN4graphene. As these kinds of studies offer detailed information for understanding the reaction mechanisms, one can foresee an acceleration of the experimental results in the field of PGM-free catalysts due to their excellent catalytic capabilities.

Xie et al.100 provided a recent and comprehensive study on Performance enhancement and degradation mechanism identification of a single-atom CoNC catalyst for proton exchange membrane fuel cells. The accuracy and reliability of the obtained results are achieved by coupling the electrochemical measurements with the well-established complementary bulk and surface characterization methods (XRD, XPS, ICP-OES, N2 adsorption, STEM, XANES and EXAFS with synchrotron radiation) and first principles DFT calculations. Thus, the authors offer a model and a guide for the use of these powerful tools in the catalysis field, in general and PEM fuel cells, in particular.

In a recently published paper, Cheng et al.101 presented a template-free method to synthesise high density iron single atoms anchored on carbon nanotubes for high temperature polymer electrolyte membrane fuel cells following the same goal: to find promising materials aiming to replace precious and expensive Pt catalysts for the ORR in PEMFCs. Their results were demonstrated in an HT-PEMFC, delivering a peak power density of 266 mW cm2 and excellent stability at 240 C using anhydrous H2 as the fuel. The authors claim that their method provides a facile and practical route for developing highly efficient PGM-free catalysts for HT-PEMFCs. A comparison with a Pt/C standard was performed with the result that their proposed sample (atomic iron homogeneously supported on CNTs with loading up to 3.5 wt%) shows increased stability and a better tolerance to PA poisoning.

3. Direct methanol fuel cells (DMFCs)

3.1 Short overview

For portable power applications, direct methanol fuel cells (DMFCs) have gained much interest since they are more viable candidates compared to PEMFCs due to the higher energy density of alcohol, compact configuration and convenient operation. There have been several studies giving an overview of the various aspects of DMFCs including membranes, diffusion layers, plates and catalysts, although many factors must be resolved before they can be commercialized.102,103 Regarding DMFCs, crossover of methanol through the membrane from the anode side to the cathode side, water loss, and water recycling are issues that affect the volume, weight, and complexity of these devices.104,105 Such factors become of greater concern as the size of the DMFC decreases. In this respect, crossover of methanol is just one of the barriers to the commercialization of DMFCs.

As already mentioned, electrocatalysts are key components of a fuel cell, on which anodic (alcohol or hydrogen oxidation) and cathodic (oxygen reduction) reactions take place. For a DMFC, optimal electrocatalysts are required to achieve high activity in methanol oxidation and the oxygen reduction reaction, for the anode and cathode, respectively; in addition, they should have good long-term stability, adequate electrical conductivity, and an affordable price. Although significant efforts are made worldwide in developing novel electrocatalysts for fuel cells, state-of-the-art systems still use expensive Pt/C catalysts even though they are prone to electrocorrosion under the operating conditions of the cell.106,107 Corrosion results in either Pt dissolution or oxidation of the carbon support leading to detachment, Ostwald ripening and agglomeration of platinum nanoparticles.108,109 The consequence of both processes is the loss of the active surface of the catalysts. In practice, this activity loss is compensated by very high Pt loadings, which keeps the price of the PEM fuel cells high.

Another issue of the traditional Pt/C catalysts is their sensitivity for CO poisoning, as it causes a significant performance loss.110 It should be noted that according to generally accepted pathways CO is supposed to be one of the intermediates of electrooxidation of different alcohols. Therefore, high tolerance to CO and other methanol oxidation byproducts is one of the criteria for the preparation of efficient catalysts for DMFCs.111 Thus, although carbon black supported Pt catalysts are commercially available in large quantities with reliable quality, it is important to explore alternative materials that can provide improved stability and increased CO tolerance. The state-of-the-art commercial CO-tolerant electrocatalyst contains PtRu alloy nanoparticles on an active carbon support.112114

In general, the introduction of a second oxophilic metal facilitates easy oxidation of the CO via the so-called bifunctional mechanism, provided the atomic closeness between the oxophilic sites and Pt is ensured.115117 However, it is well known that oxophilic metal-containing catalysts become unstable at high potentials, which leads to serious deterioration of the catalytic activity.117

Thus, the major drawback of DMFC commercialization is the lack of an inexpensive catalyst and membrane with sufficiently high and stable performance. It is necessary to mention that with regard to the catalysts, most of the reviews mainly summarize the results of testing electrocatalysts under the given operating conditions, and have paid less attention to the design and the methods of preparation of catalysts and their supports. In the literature there is a huge amount of research devoted to the development of new electrocatalytic systems that can be promising candidates for use in DMFCs. However, most of these studies are carried out in a standard three-electrode electrochemical cell. This section will summarize the performance of various Pt-based electrocatalysts evaluated using DMFC tests.

There are two basic types of DMFCs: passive and active DMFCs.102,118 An active system of DMFC is suitable for portable applications due to its high power output. Compared to the active DMFC, the self-breathing DMFC with an open cathode structure has many unique advantages such as simple structure, small size, light weight, high energy density, low operating temperature, and high security, since the use of the complex auxiliary equipment system can be avoided.119 It is highly competitive in the small-scale energy market.

3.2 Noble metal-based catalysts used in passive system DMFCs

Passive DMFCs operate without the help of external devices for pumping methanol and blowing air into the cell. Oxygen thus diffuses into the cathode from the ambient air by a kind of air-breathing action of the cell, and methanol diffuses into the anode from a built-in feed reservoir driven by a concentration gradient between the anode and the reservoir.118,120

However, it also has several disadvantages, such as poor discharge performance and poor stability, which prevent it from being widely used in commercial DMFC applications. Ensuring long-term stable operation of the DMFC is absolutely essential for commercial applications, and the reason for performance degradation is a key research point influenced by Membrane Electrode Assembly (MEA) preparation and operating conditions. Unsupported catalysts or metal blacks are mainly utilized in systems operating in passive mode, that is, without any air blowers or fuel circulating pumps, such as in portable electronic applications.121123

Several examples of new types of electrocatalysts used in passive system DMFCs are presented in Table 3.

As was mentioned above, nanostructured PtRu material is considered as the best catalyst for DMFCs, but the performance decay resulting from Ru loss seriously hinders the commercial application. The results presented in ref. 119 showed that Ru dispersion, membrane swelling and water flooding were the main reasons for the performance decline of the PtRu black-JM anode. It has been demonstrated that the degradation of Pt black-JM, used as the cathode, was less serious than that of the anode and it could be recovered. In this study, the traditional equivalent circuit model of self-breathing DMFCs was modified according to the fact that the dielectric relaxation phenomenon of porous electrodes occurred at the cathode rather than at the anode.

In the preparation of the MEA, simply adding nanoparticles of magnesium oxide as a sacrificial template to the catalytic layer (CL) and the microporous layer (MPL) in the anode led, after dissolution of MgO in acidic solution, to the formation of a porous structure of the anode.124 The improved performance of the novel MEA can be ascribed to the increased electrochemical surface area, decreased charge-transfer resistance as well as efficient mass transfer of methanol after the formation of the porous structure in the anode. The present work provides a very simple but very effective way to reduce the dosage of the noble metal catalysts for fuel cells.

The membranes constructed from sodium dodecylbenzenesulfonate adsorbed holey graphene oxides (SDBS-HGO)s and used as PEMs for air-breathing DMFC applications showed much higher electrochemical performance and better stability than that with Nafion 112.125

The better performance of the SDBS-HGO paper was attributed to the specific holey structure of graphene oxide (see Scheme 1), which provides comparable proton conductivity and lower methanol permeability in comparison to the commercial Nafion 112.


Scheme 1 Schematic illustration of the synthetic procedure of the SDBS-HGO paper. Reproduced with permission from ref. 125, Copyright 2014 The Royal Society of Chemistry.

Chen et al. demonstrated the preparation by the electrospinning technique of novel MEAs with a nanofiber network catalytic layer (NNCL) structure in the anode of a passive DMFC, which resulted in significant reduction in noble metal catalyst loading.126

It should be noted that electrospinning is a very useful technique that allows the formation of continuous nanofibers with high surface area, remarkable porosity, permeability, superior chemical stability, high strength, mechanical flexibility, etc.127 The experimental design for the electrospinning technique used to fabricate the nanofiber mat electrode is presented in Scheme 2.128


Scheme 2 Experimental design for electrospinning a nanofiber catalyst mat. A percolating network of Pt/C particles is contained within a nanofiber by a proton-conducting polymer binder. The nanofibers are collected as a mat on carbon paper or directly on a collector drum. Reproduced with permission from ref. 128, Copyright 2011 Wiley Publications.

When the PtRu (1:1) loading within the NNCL was 1.0 mg cm2, the maximum power density of a DMFC was even slightly higher than that with a conventional MEA with a PtRu loading of 2.0 mg cm2. Electrochemical tests show that such an NNCL exhibited a great increase in catalyst utilization and a decrease in the charge-transfer resistance of the anode in comparison with the conventional MEA.

The results of the optimization process involving a screening process followed by response surface methodology (RSM) were used for the single DMFC performance testing of the PtRu/TiO2-CNF anode electrocatalyst.129 TiO2 and carbon-containing composite support with a TiO2-CNF nanofibre structure was prepared via an electrospinning technique.134 The optimum parameters suggested by RSM are a PtRu composition of 30.25 wt% and a catalyst loading of 0.59 mg cm2, resulting in almost perfect agreement between the measured current density (603.06 mA mgPtRu1) and the predicted value (600.63 mA mgPtRu1). The current density obtained in this study is the highest among other research studies in the same field.

From an economic viewpoint, the actual cost of Sn, which can also be used as a second oxophilic metal, is much lower than that of Pt or Ru. PtSn/C-PANI electrocatalysts with different Pt/Sn atomic ratios supported on polyaniline fiber (PANI)-containing Vulcan-polyaniline (C-PANI) composite were synthesized in ref. 130. Use of the PtSn (70:30)/C-PANI as an alloy type anode catalyst, instead of commercial PtRu/C, in a passive DMFC resulted in decrease of methanol crossover by about 30% and displayed significant CO tolerance as well as high electrocatalytic activity and stability toward methanol oxidation.

In ref. 131 nano-sized MnO2, used as a co-catalyst, was deposited on reduced graphene sheets along with Pt nanoparticles. In situ carbonization using L-ascorbic acid as the carbon source was employed to boost the performance of the catalyst, where the carbon riveted PtMnO2/rGO-L catalyst showed not only larger electrochemical surface area and better catalytic activity towards methanol oxidation, but also revealed stronger ability to resist CO poisoning and better stability than PtMnO2/rGO and Pt/rGO catalysts. The improvement of the catalyst performance can be attributed to the synergistic effect that developed between MnO2 and Pt and the anchoring effect of L-ascorbic acid.

It should be noted that in the case of a passive micro DMFC (DMFC), most of the research work has focused only on the evaluation of structural aspects and performance-related operating conditions. Such studies are usually carried out on commercially available PtRu (anode) and Pt (cathode) catalysts. Thus, Wang et al.132 recently systematically investigated the influence of many variables, including the hot-press conditions, current collector channel patterns, current collector open ratios, and their effect on passive DMFC performance through experimentation and simulation.

In ref. 133, a tailored and low-cost MEA, using the materials available in the market, was proposed to achieve higher performances working under higher methanol concentrations. Despite the reduced loadings of both anode and cathode catalysts (PtRu: 3 mg cm2 and Pt: 1.3 mg cm2, respectively), the maximum power density of 3.00 mW cm2 was achieved using a methanol concentration of 5 M and carbon cloth with a dual-layer structure, as the anode diffusion layer.

The optimized fabrication, assembly and operation parameters shed light on the design considerations necessary for the wide adaptation of high-performance and durable DMFCs for portable applications.

3.3 Noble metal-based catalysts used in active system DMFCs

3.3.1 Challenges in using PtRu-based electrocatalysts. Effect of the type of carbonaceous materials. In this subsection the most important challenges arising from the use of Pt-based electrocatalysts in the active type of DMFCs are summarized. These results were obtained using commercially available and in-house developed PtRu catalysts on the anode side and Pt on the cathode side. Features of the application of unsupported and carbon supported PtRu anode catalysts as well as the influence of the type of carbonaceous support materials on the DMFC performance are presented in Table 4.

The micro direct methanol fuel cell (MicroDMFC) is an emerging technology due to its special potential for portable applications.120 The maximum power output obtained at room temperature using an active metallic MicroDMFC with an active area of 2.25 cm2 was 18.1 mW cm2 for a hot-pressed MEA using materials purchased from Quintech with very low catalyst loading (3 mg cm2 PtRu black at the anode side and 0.5 mg cm2 Pt black at the cathode side) costing around 15 euros.135

The effect of the organic solvent polarity on the performance of the catalyst layers prepared with them for methanol electrooxidation has been studied.136 The current densities for PtRu black anode catalyst layers in single DMFCs are higher when the solvent is n-butyl acetate (NBA), the mass transport limitations being much more apparent with 2-propanol (IPA). It has been demonstrated that the proton resistance for NBA is significantly lower than for IPA, thus suggesting that the larger number of accessible active sites for methanol oxidation in the former are well connected to the Nafion ionomers and more easily transported to the membrane.

The results presented in ref. 137 indicated that the agglomeration of the metal particles of the commercial PtRu/C-JM anode electrocatalysts was more serious than that of the Pt/C-JM cathode. About 30% of the maximum power density was lost after the 75 h life cycle test, compared with the initial peak value of 66 mW cm2. The results indicated that the agglomeration of electrocatalysts and the delamination of the MEA concurrently contribute to the performance degradation of the DMFC.

The results presented in Table 4 also demonstrate the importance of choosing the most efficient loading method for Pt and Ru, leading to a high degree of alloying and homogeneous distribution over a carbon support.

PtRu black and PtRu/C with a nominal Pt:Ru atomic ratio of 1:1 have been prepared by a modified polyol process (co-reduction of metal precursor salts with ethylene glycol) for DMFCs, and the effects of the carbon support, alloying degree and unalloyed Ru on the catalysts' activities have been investigated.138 It has been demonstrated that without the carbon support, PtRu nanoparticles tend to agglomerate, while the PtRu nanoparticles in PtRu/C have a good dispersion.

PtRu/C nanocatalyst prepared by maintaining the molar ratio of citric acid (CA) to platinum and metallic ruthenium salts CA/PtRu = 1:1, gave a smaller particle size (2.6 nm) with a homogeneous distribution over a carbon support compared to experiments performed with an increased CA content (CA/PtRu = 2:1, 3:1, 4:1).139 Testing of the PtRu/C catalysts at the anode of a DMFC indicated that the in-house developed PtRu/C nanocatalyst gave a slightly higher performance than did the commercial E-TEK catalyst.

It has been demonstrated by Xu et al.140 that the activity of the nanostructured PtRu/C catalyst prepared from a water-in-oil pseudomicroemulsion with the aqueous phase of a mixed concentrated solution of H2PtCl6, RuCl3 and carbon powder, oil phase of cyclohexane, ionic surfactant of sodium dodecylbenzene sulfonate (C18H29NaO3S) and co-surfactant n-butanol is much higher than that of PtRu nanoparticles prepared with nonionic surfactants.

The importance of using novel carbonaceous support materials with appropriate porosity, high specific surface area and good conductivity to provide superior stability and corrosion resistance to electrocatalysts has already been mentioned in Section 2.2.

In ref. 141, different commercially available carbons have been tested with respect to both thermal and electrochemical activity/stability as support materials for dispersed Pt nanoparticles. Despite the fact that the best results for the ORR activity and stability tests were obtained on Pt catalysts supported on mesoporous graphitized nanoparticles (GNP500) and ordered mesoporous carbon (OMC), from a practical and financial point of view, the relatively cheap spherical mesoporous GNP500 was preferred to the very expensive two-dimensional structure of the OMC.

Better performance of the mesoporous carbon supported PtRu catalyst compared to PtRu/Vulcan, exhibiting a wider range of operating current, was demonstrated by Bruno et al.142 Moreover, an improvement in the mass transport was observed for the catalyst supported on mesoporous carbon, yielding a lower voltage drop at high current density.

Carbon nanotubes (CNTs) and carbon nanofibers (CNFs) can also be used efficiently as metal supports in fuel cell electrodes in order to replace the classically used carbon blacks.143 Because of their special structure, CNFs combine two properties that rarely coexist in a material: high mesoporosity and high electrical conductivity.144

Vapour-grown carbon fibers (VGCFs) were added to the anode catalyst layer of a DMFC to improve the cell performance through structural modification of the catalyst layer.145 A catalyst layer with 2 wt% VGCF loading showed the best cell performance. The nanosized scale carbon fibers provided a high surface area with low micropore content. This can enhance mass transport through the electrocatalyst layers, which in turn enhances the electrochemical performance.

It has been proposed that VGCF can be effective in increasing the amount of catalyst reaction sites, possibly by reducing the agglomeration of catalyst particles.

CNF with a herringbone-type structure, which is characterized by the alignment of graphene symmetrically angled to the fiber axis, was selected as the catalyst support because it has an edge-rich surface and a high surface area.146

In the metal dissolution test, the PtRu (70 wt%)/CNF catalysts showed 1.52 times lower Ru leaching than a tested commercial PtRu/C (74.0 wt%, Johnson Matthey) catalyst. The results of long-term performance tests also prove the higher durability of the anode catalyst compared with the commercial catalyst, when the anode polarization is compared before and after operation for 2000 h.

Three types of CNFs with very different surface structures, such as platelet, herringbone and tubular ones, were prepared by a microwave-polyol method.147 It was found that the methanol fuel cell activities of CNF supported PtRu (24 1 wt%) alloy-type catalysts were in the order of platelet > tubular > herringbone. The best electrocatalytic activity was obtained for the platelet CNF, which is characterized by its edge surface and high graphitization degree.

Ordered uniform porous carbon frameworks with pore sizes in the range of 10 to 1000 nm used as supports for a Pt50Ru50 alloy catalyst were synthesized against removable colloidal silica crystalline templates by the carbonization of phenol and formaldehyde as a carbon precursor as shown in Scheme 3.148 The porous carbons with about 25 nm in pore diameter showed the highest performance with power densities of 58 and 167 mW cm2 at 30 and 70 C, respectively; these values roughly correspond to 70 and 40% increase as compared to those of a commercially available PtRu alloy catalyst (E-TEK), respectively.


Scheme 3 Synthetic procedure for uniform porous carbons of tunable pore sizes through the colloidal crystal template approach. Reproduced with permission from ref. 148, Copyright 2004 American Chemical Society.

In order to better understand the influence of the atomic structure of various carbon supports on the catalytic activity of PtRu in relation to the methanol oxidation reaction, PtRu nanoparticles on the multi-walled carbon nanotubes (MWCNTs) and on the Vulcan XC-72 carbon were synthesized using a simple sodium borohydride reduction method with the surfactant tetraoctylammonium bromide (TOAB).149 The DMFC performance test data showed that PtRu/MWCNT catalysts yielded about 3539% higher power densities than the PtRu/Vulcan. It has been demonstrated by Prabhuram et al.150 that the PtRu/MWCNT nanocatalyst used at the anode of the fuel cell yielded higher performance than that obtained on the commercial E-TEK PtRu/C catalyst.

Fuel cell testing of PtRu catalysts supported on differently prepared and pretreated carbon nanotubes was done by Tokarz et al.151 The performance of the PtRu catalysts supported on multiwall carbon nanotubes (MCNTs) treated by either high-temperature KOH etching or annealing at 2500 C (graphitization) was compared to a commercial PtRu catalyst supported on a high-surface-area carbon black. Among the different MCNT supports, the graphitized, entangled support offered the best performance in all tests, which was equal to the performance of the commercial catalyst, although the MCNT catalyst layer was ca. 2.2 times thicker than the carbon black catalyst layer. Even for an MCNT catalyst layer, which was almost 7 times thicker than the carbon black catalyst layer, the transport limitations were not prohibitive. This confirmed the expected potential of nanotube supports for providing superior reactant transport properties of the catalyst layers in the acid type PEM fuel cells.

Fabrication of carbon capsules with hollow core/mesoporous shell (HCMS) was reported by replication through nanocasting of solid core/mesoporous shell silica particles.152 HCMS carbon capsules have an interconnected bimodal pore system composed of a hollow macroporous core and mesoporous shell and have higher surface area and pore volume compared to the aforementioned porous carbon systems, which are potentially important for support materials.

The HCMS carbon supported Pt50Ru50 catalysts exhibited much higher specific activity for methanol oxidation than the commonly used E-TEK catalyst by about 80%, proving that the HCMS carbon capsules are an excellent support for electrode catalysts in DMFCs.

The electrochemical characterization showed that the PtRu catalysts supported on carbons functionalized with sulfuric acid (SA: H2SO4) and chlorosulfuric acid (C-SA: HSO3Cl) exhibited better performance for methanol/ethanol oxidation if compared to those using unfunctionalized supports.153 The observed better performance can be mainly attributed to the easier access to protons (higher ionic transport), better wettability (higher water uptake), and better distribution of the nanoparticles over the support (higher platinum utilization); indicating that sulfonation is an efficient approach to functionalize, improve the performance, and reduce the cost of fuel cell catalysts.

Thus, the best commercially available anode catalysts still belong to the PtRu/C family, which are known to suffer from carbon electrocorrosion, dissolution of Ru and Pt under the operating conditions and weak bonding between the metals and the support. This is the reason that a huge amount of research is still directed towards improving the properties of these catalytic systems using a variety of carbonaceous materials such as carbon black, mesoporous carbon, CNFs, CNTs, MWCNTs and graphene.

In this subsection the most important factors that influence the catalytic performances of anode electrocatalysts in DMFCs are presented using the simplest PtRu/C catalytic systems. When developing novel anode electrocatalysts for DMFC applications all these aspects must be taken into account.

3.3.2 Nitrogen- and boron-doped carbon-based electrocatalysts. Nitrogen-doped carbon materials can undergo a specific strong interaction with Pt and help to reduce the absorbance of CO on Pt particles, which is a major factor leading to the poisoning of the Pt catalyst. Recent and earlier results demonstrating the effect of doping carbonaceous materials with nitrogen or boron on the DMFC performance are summarized in Table 5.
Table 5 Comparison of active DMFC performances with different anode electrocatalysts. Nitrogen- and boron-doped carbon-based electrocatalystsa
