As shown in Table 10, alloying Pt with Ru brings several advantages, for instance, high activity due to the low particle size, which influences the DEFC performance.351,352 Ma et al.352 confirmed that alloying Pd and Ru is also a great choice. They have designed a fuel cell using a MnO2 nanotube catalyst prepared in-house as the cathode and A201 as the AEM. In comparison with a Pd/C catalyst with the same particle size, Pd3Ru/C was 1.8 times superior in the practical fuel cell with a PPD of 176 mW cm2 at 80 C.

A Pt anode alloyed with Au for DEFCs shows increased stability, but did not present any improvement in ethanol electroactivity. However, Xu et al.353 demonstrated that Pd behaves differently than Pt when alloyed with Au and improves the electro-oxidation of ethanol, thus resulting in more stable DEFCs. This behaviour can be due to the special ability of Au to withdraw electrons from the neighbouring Pd atoms. In the DEFC, the stability increased with the increase of Au content, but was in detriment of the activity, therefore a compromise between the two features should be done. The authors concluded that the composition Pd3Au/C maintains a good cell performance (53.8 mW cm2 at 40 C) equivalent to that of the Pd/C anode, and at the same time improves the stability of the DEFC (a loss of 25% in current density after 3000 cycles compared with 53% loss when Pd/C was used, under the same conditions).

Considering that the MEA assembly of AEM DEFCs was derived from acid DAFCs, Li et al.354 tried to increase the performance of DEFCs by improving the anode electrode structure. Thus, the DEFC fabricated using a non-platinum FeCo HYPERMEC K14 (Acta) cathode, a A-201 membrane provided by Tokuyama, and an in-house prepared anode (PdNi/C) achieved a power density of 130 mW cm2 at 80 C at a current density of 1060 mA cm2. The difference between the conventional anode and the integrated anode structure proposed consists in adopting a modified way of putting the catalysts in contact with the Ni foam.

The Ni foam skeleton was dip-coated in a solution containing the catalyst and the ink, while the conventional method involves brushing of the Ni foam with the prepared catalyst ink. Changing the method leads to a good dispersion of the catalyst onto the Ni foam, an enhanced transport of reactants/products and ion charges and a reduced cell resistance. The same group tried a layered reduction method when Pd was deposited on the Ni foam, and succeeded in increasing the DEFC performance to 164 mW cm2.375

In 2011, An et al.379 demonstrated that very good performances can also be obtained if, instead of the anion exchange membrane, a cation exchange membrane to conduct Na+ (found in the fuel stream) from the anode to the cathode is used. The DEFC assembly consists of a PdNi/C anode, FeCo K-14 HYPERMEC (ACTA) cathode and AEM or CEM as the exchange membrane. The performance of the CEM is slightly higher (135 mW cm2) than that of AEM (115 mW cm2) at 90 C, and seems to be more stable with temperature (AEM starts to degrade at temperatures >60 C). However, using a cation exchange membrane also brings other disadvantages that need to be addressed, like NaOH formation at the cathode.

Not only alloying Pd, but also addition of a metal oxide could boost the DEFC performance. It has been assumed that the oxophilicity of the metal oxides has the ability to reduce the Pd loading and to provide the hydroxyl groups required for the oxidation of ethanol molecules adsorbed onto the surface.360 Supporting the Pd nanoparticles on CeO2, yields twice as much higher performance of the DEFC (140 mW cm2), compared with the conventional Pd/C and confirms that PdOHads species formed on the ceria support are responsible for the enhanced DEFC performance.365 Trimetallic anodes (PdNiZn) were also investigated in passive DEFCs having a polymer electrolyte membrane and FeCo K-14 Hypermec as the cathode. The PEM-DEFC performance was high (170 mW cm2) and was associated with the synergy between Ni and Zn, which promoted the increase of OHads species on the catalyst surface.374

Very good performance of the DEFC was obtained by replacing the conventional Hypermec or Pt/C cathode with a modified PdAu alloy supported on carbon nanotubes as the cathode (185 mW cm2).376 Also, on modifying the carbon support with nitrogen, when Pd was used as the anode, excellent performance of bthe DEFC at 60 C was obtained (a PPD of 202 mW cm2).370 This increase in power density is explained by a higher dispersion of Pd nanoparticles when the support is decorated with nitrogen. Similar results (202.3 mW cm2) were obtained by using a bimetallic PdAu anode supported on carbon nanotubes with the note that higher temperature (i.e., 80 C) was used.359

4.3.3 Graphene based support for electrocatalysts used for DEFCs. To further increase the applicability of the materials used as anodes or cathodes described in the previous sections, the nanoparticles of noble metals are dispersed on high surface area supports, like carbon black or carbon nanotubes. Recently, graphenes and their derivatives belonging to this class of materials were found as promising alternatives to the classical carbon-based supports. Therefore, as a strategy to minimize the metal-loading and to improve the electro-oxidation of ethanol toward commercialization of DEFCs, graphene-like materials have been increasingly considered as supports. We will further discuss the design improvements in graphene-based electrodes for ethanol electro-oxidation.
Graphenes. Graphenes are 2D materials with single or few atomic layers, high surface area, good electrical conductivity and chemical activity.380382 With the progress in the field of graphene materials and the developed properties, they have been applied in fuel cell as supports for anodes and the major improvement is better metal dispersion in contact with the support, which induces much higher electroactivity compared with the analogues supported on carbon black.34,383,384 Several studies underlined the advantages of metals supported on graphene, such as Pt380,381,385 or Pd,382,386 however many others emphasized that alloying the metal will bring more important gains when used for the ethanol electro-oxidation reaction. Thus, PtPd,387 PdAg,388,389 PdNi,360 NiCo,390 and even trimetallic combinations like PtRhFe391 pointed out to an improvement of the electrocatalysts for the ethanol electro-oxidation. Involvement of two metals has proven to result in several favourable outcomes such as good catalytic activity, better stability and also different selectivity. All these features are related to different phenomena when the two metals are associated, such as modified geometry of the active site, changed electronic state, improved stability and the presence of a synergetic or bi-functional effect.392

Nevertheless, the use of only graphene as the support for the metal nanoparticles has proven to not completely address the issues concerning the high dispersibility of the metallic nanoparticles during the deposition process. Thus, the next step was to modify graphene in order to improve the anodes in alkaline DEFCs.


Modified graphenes. Graphene can be modified in various ways in order to improve the performance of the direct ethanol fuel cell. For this purpose different molecules can be used to modify the graphene surface and can be classified into non-covalent and covalent functions of the bond type formed with the surface.393

The presence of functional groups on graphene nanosheets was found to increase the wettability and the accessibility of the metal nanoparticles to the electroactive surface, by acting as nucleation centers and anchoring sites and thus limiting the particle growth.394396

Moreover, using graphene oxides as supports, due to the presence of OH groups on the surface, a composite with the functionalized group and metallic nanoparticles is formed. Such a composite, owing to its triple junction structure, has an increased surface area, and better metal dispersion and electrical conductivity.382,397


Doped graphenes. Doping graphene with other hetero-atoms was among the first attempts to improve its properties. It was found that insertion of nitrogen in graphene produces structural changes and electronic rearrangement of the graphene sheets, which induces the appearance of new active sites in the graphene structure.398400 Additionally, several other benefits were confirmed during different studies: N-doping can reduce the particle size, improve the anchoring of the catalysts onto the electrode, and assure a greater dispersion of the nanoparticles, all these resulting in an electrocatalyst with higher performance. For example, Wu et al.399 demonstrated that N-doped graphene generates a synergetic effect with the supported metal, which results in four times increased electro-oxidation activity for PdPb/NG (152.3 mA cm2) compared with Pd/G (38.0 mA cm2). The main reason for this behaviour is the very good dispersibility of the metals when the support contains a large amount of nitrogen as can be seen in Fig. 19. In another attempt, insertion of two heterogeneous atoms, B and N, into graphene was tried and it succeeded in improving the electrochemical activity and stability of the electrodes in the ethanol electro-oxidation. Also, Chowdhury et al.401 reported a Pd/BN-GNR modified electrocatalyst prepared in one-step with improved performance compared to Pd/GNRs and Pd/C in ethanol electro-oxidation (see Fig. 19). All these reports claim that the synergy between the co-doped/doped-graphene and the metallic nanoparticles is responsible for the enhancement of the electroactivity due to the good dispersibility of the metal onto the surface, increase of catalytic active sites and increase of conductivity.
Fig. 19 (a) TEM images of Pd/G (A1/A2) and Pd/NG (B1/B2) and the particle size distribution of Pd/G (A3) and Pd/NG (B3); (b) CO stripping curves on Pd/G, Pd/NG and PdPb/NG (8:1.0) in 1 M KOH solution. Scan rate: 50 mV s1; (c) cyclic voltammograms of Pd/C, PdPb/C (8:1.0), Pd/G, Pd/NG, PdPb/G (8:1.0) and PdPb/NG (8:1.0) in the 1 M KOH + 1 M C2H5OH solution. Scan rate: 50 mV s1;399 (d) schematic illustration of fabrication for Pd/BN-GNRs; TEM images of (e) Pd/GNRs and (f) Pd/BN-GNRs, inset shows the size distribution of the Pd NPs; (g) CV of Pd-based catalysts in 1.0 M NaOH + 1.0 M C2H5OH solution at a scan rate of 50 mV s1, (h) chronoamperometric curves for the Pd-based catalysts in 1.0 M NaOH + 1.0 M C2H5OH solution at 0.20 V.402 Reproduced with permissions from ref. 399 and 402, respectively, Copyrights 2015 and 2016, respectively, Elsevier Publications and The Royal Society of Chemistry.

Nanocomposites: metal oxidesgraphenes. Previous studies have shown that the combination of noble metals with metal oxides yields more active and stable electrocatalysts for the ethanol electro-oxidation reaction.403407 The main disadvantage of these electrocatalysts is related to the poor conductivity of metal oxides, which hinders their use in the application as supports. One way to surpass this issue is to design electrocatalysts that take the advantage of the good electronic conductivity of graphenes. Some examples of such triple composition noble metals/metallic oxides/graphene are reviewed below.

The first attempt to improve the properties of graphene by adding metal oxides to form a nanocomposite was made by Wen et al.382 They succeeded in preparing a new composite by combining the properties of SnO2, graphene oxide and a Pd precursor (Pd/SnO2-GNS). The Pd/SnO2-GNS presents higher catalytic activity compared with the Pd/GNS catalyst in ethanol oxidation, and this enhanced activity was due to the high ECSA. The good dispersibility of Pd particles when the SnO2-GNS support vs. GNS was used has been emphasized by TEM analysis (see Fig. 20).


Fig. 20 TEM images of (a) GO, (b) SnO2-GNS, (c) Pd/GNS and (d) Pd/SnO2-GNS. The inset image of (b) is the high-resolution TEM image of SnO2-GNS. Reproduced with permission from ref. 382, Copyright 2013 Elsevier Publications.

Few years later, Qu et al.408 found a way to improve the electro-oxidation activity of noble metals deposited on SnO2/graphene by designing a layer-by-layer SnO2/Pt/graphene anode using a mild microwave assisted polyol process (MAPP). They have also shown that the electro-oxidation process of ethanol depends on the amount of graphene deposited. 30 wt% of graphene was found to be the optimum amount for the developed compositions.

Cerium oxide was also considered to improve the activity of Me/graphene electrocatalysts.409 Thus, the strategy chosen to improve the electroactivity was to alloy Pt with cerium oxide in order to reduce the amount of Pt, together with the modification of the reduced graphene surface by anchoring SO3H groups. The role of sulfonic groups on the graphene surface was to improve the Pt dispersion by increasing the hydrophilicity of the support. The results showed that the employed approach was successful with an increase of 17% of current density comparing Pt/CeO2/SRGO with Pt/SRGO, which is in agreement with the proposed Tempkin mechanism.

The use of manganese oxide and a bimetallic alloy (PdNiMnO2/rGO)410 or of a magnetic spinel in a composition of the type Pd/CoFe2O4/rGO presents a corroborative effect in the presence of the metal and graphene support and yields electrocatalysts with higher stability and CO tolerance and also with enhanced electroactivity.

Shamraiz et al.411 decided to modify the Pd d-band centers with earth-abundant alkaline metals. According to the d-band theory, the Pd adsorption capacity is enhanced with the uplift of d-band centers and therefore using earth-alkaline metals, like Ca, which possesses a higher lattice constant (5.58 ) than Pd (3.89 ) and has the potential to uplift the Pd d-band centers. Such changes in d-band will produce improvement in the oxidation of alcohols at more negative potentials. Undoubtedly, the presence of oxygen from CaO during the doping process helped in overcoming the adsorbed intermediate poisoning issues occurring on carbonaceous materials. The enhanced PdCa/rGO mass activity (4674.70 mA mg1 Pd) compared with Pd/rGO (1490.08 mA mg1 Pd) was also related to the homogeneous distribution of Ca and Pd on graphene sheets which resulted in high activity, stability and increased tolerance to CO poisoning of the electrocatalyst.


Functionalized graphenes. Noncovalent anchors, for noble metal modified graphenes, can boost the ethanol electro-oxidation reaction. In the following section examples such as poly(diallyldimethylammonium chloride) (PDDA), perylene tetracarboxylic acid (PTCA), poly(3,4-ethylenedioxythiophene) (PEDOT), dimethyldiallylammonium chloride (DMDAAC), polyaniline (PANI), and polypyrrole (PPy) are reviewed. In Fig. 21 some examples of the synthesis method of polymer/graphene based electrocatalysts are schematically represented.
Fig. 21 Schematic representation of the synthesis of (a) PdPDDA/rGO,412 (b) Pd/DMDAACrGO,397 (c) PtSn/PEDOT/EGO,415 and (d) PtPd/PPy/NGO.420 Reproduced with permissions from ref. 412, 397, 415, and 420, Copyrights 2015, 2016, 2018 and 2018, respectively, Elsevier Publications.

PDDA was used to design and prepare a monometallic composite like PdPDDA/rGO412 or a bimetallic composite electrode such as PDDArGOPdPt via the sonoelectrochemical method.413 The use of PDDA in both cases increased the ethanol electro-oxidation and the stability of the electrocatalyst mainly due to the good dispersion of PDDA and the metal onto the graphene surface. The current density of PdPDDA/rGO developed for ethanol oxidation is 1.38 times higher than that of the Pd/rGO homologue,412 while for PDDArGOPdPt the current density (6.7 mA cm2) is nearly 1.5 times higher than that of the PdPt alloy without rGO.413 The high homogeneity of PDDA has also been found to increase the graphene conductivity and to improve the CO tolerance. Moreover, the sonoelectrochemical method used by Shi et al.413 plays an important role in graphene sheet separation, thus combining the special feature of 2D graphene with the metallic alloy properties.

Li et al.414 studied the influence of PTCA when coating the graphene sheets. The authors demonstrated the successful functionalization of graphene with PTCA taking advantage of stacking and hydrophobic forces. The Pd/PTCAGS (NaBH4) electrocatalyst prepared presented enhanced electroactivity for ethanol electro-oxidation, which can be mainly correlated with the narrower size distribution, better homogeneity and uniform crystal structure. Several studies415418 reported an increased electroactivity when PEDOT was used as the polymer to obtain graphene nanocomposites. Its success is mainly due to its high conductivity and very good stability in the oxidized state in comparison with other conductive polymers. Pt/PEDOT/ER-GO composites present a maximum mass peak current density of 78.7% which was better than that of the Pt/C electrode.418 The advantage of this composite is the long-term stability in acidic medium and very good tolerance to CO poisoning but also the fact that it is prepared by a greener route416 in order to increase its applicability.415 Studies on PEDOT/rGO were extended to the use of Pd as the noble metal417 and the big plus was the one-pot facile synthesis of the nanocomposites, since their synthesis can be complex and laborious. Such materials are found to be very stable and have enhanced electrocatalytic activity compared with PdPEDOT and Pd/C catalysts.

Zhang et al.397 demonstrated that the use of DMDAAC, a cationic polymer, has several advantages: it stabilizes the metallic nanoparticles, avoids graphene nanosheet agglomeration and increases the conductivity. It has been found that Pd/DMDAAC-rGO has 7 times higher electrocatalytic activity and stability (3.25 102 mA cm2 exchanged current density) than Pd/rGO for ethanol oxidation (4.87 103 mA cm2).397 One of the main reasons for this behaviour is that DMDAAC assists the formation of small Pd nanoparticles and better dispersion at high metal concentrations, when it is supported on rGO.

Recently, polyaniline and polypyrrole were employed to enhance the performance of electrocatalysts for ethanol electro-oxidation. Besides the good conductivity and stability, polyaniline is also known have good intrinsic flexibility.419 The insertion of PANI into the Au/PANI/rGO electrode, by a facile and rapid strategy, will increase the peak current density by 3.5 times when compared with the Au/rGO electrode, probably due to a better charge transport phenomenon. A similar effect was found when PPy was used as an anchor for the deposition of the catalysts onto the electrode.

Ni nanoparticles421 or bimetallic PtPd noble metals420 were used as noble metals in combination with PPy and graphene oxide to produce electrocatalysts for ethanol electro-oxidation. Both material combinations, besides the favourable effect over ethanol electro-oxidation (21.1 mA cm2 current density for NiNPs-R/PPy/CPE),421 present good tolerance to CO and other intermediates resulting from the ethanol oxidation.

4.4 Alkalineacid direct ethanol fuel cells (AA-DEFCs)

As described above, the conventional DEFCs operating in both acid and alkaline media, show several limitations, such as: (i) the sluggish kinetics of the EOR in the case of PEM-DEFCs; (ii) about one order of magnitude lower ionic conductivity of the AEM compared to that of Nafion membranes; (iii) poor thermal stability of the AEM, and (iv) the low theoretical voltage (1.14 V). The increase of theoretical voltage can be achieved by changing the conventional oxidant (O2/air) to a better one (H2O2). Thus, as shown in Fig. 15, while the anode potentials of both the PEM- and AEM-DEFC operating with H2O2 (as the oxidant) remain the same (i.e., 0.09 V for PEM-DEFC and 0.74 V for AEM-DEFC), the cathode potentials of both the PEM- and AEM-DEFC increase from 1.23 V to 1.78 V and from 0.40 V to 0.87 V, respectively.270 Based on these aspects, combining the lower anode potential of the AEM-DEFC and the higher cathode potential of PEM-DEFC, at the same time with the use of hydrogen peroxide as the oxidant, will result in the formation of a new cell characterized by a higher theoretical voltage (2.52 V) and faster kinetics of the ethanol oxidation reaction. The working principle of this fuel cell will be detailed in the next section.

The use of hydrogen peroxide, however, implies the appearance of three major issues that reduce the performance. The first one refers to the drastic drop in the cathode potential due to the H2O2 decomposition. This decomposition process led to the formation of H2O and O2, which further creates the premise for simultaneous appearance of both hydrogen peroxide reduction reaction (HPRR) and oxygen reduction reaction at the cathode. As the potential associated with each reaction is different (lower in the case of the ORR than that of the HPRR), a mixed potential is created. The second issue is caused by the release of gaseous O2 (through H2O2 decomposition) which led to a two-phase mass transport in the cathode (the mass transport resistance of liquid-phase H2O2 in the cathode electrode is much larger than that of gaseous O2) and an increase in the gas void fraction, which lower the effective active area.422 In the same time, the H2O2 decomposition will cause a large concentration loss in the cathode catalyst layer. The third issue refers to the use of metal catalysts, such as platinum, palladium or gold which are required in order to minimize/lower the rate of the homogeneous decomposition of H2O2.423 It must be noted that decomposition of H2O2 can proceed homogeneously and heterogeneously. Homogeneous decomposition is caused by the intrinsic instability of H2O2 and the rate depends on several parameters, such as temperature, concentration and the solution pH value, among other minor factors. The rate of heterogeneous decomposition, however, depends on the specific catalyst that is used at the cathode. Thus, it was experimentally shown that the best metal catalysts, which reduce the rate of homogeneous decomposition, are mainly the noble metal-based ones.20 The use of such type of metals will maintain the total cost of the AA-DEFCs high and, thus, is yet seen as an issue and future studies are required to overcome it.

In 2011, An et al.270 reported for the first time the development of a new type of DEFC, named alkalineacid DEFC (AA-DEFC), which was composed of an alkaline anode (PdNi/C with a loading of 1.0 mg cm2), a membrane (a sodium conducting membrane based on pre-treated Nafion 117) and an acid cathode (60 wt% Pt/C from Johnson-Matthey with a loading of 3.9 mg cm2). The anolyte was an aqueous solution of ethanol (3 M) and sodium hydroxide (5 M), while the catholyte was an aqueous solution of hydrogen peroxide (4 M) and sulfuric acid (1 M), both flowing at 2 mL min1 through the flow channels present at the anode and cathode, respectively. The fuel cell was able to achieve a PPD of 240 mW cm2 at 60 C with an OCV of 1.6 V. The large difference between this OCV and theoretical voltage (2.52 V) was mainly caused by the mixed potential phenomenon occurring on the cathode.

In the same year, An et al.271 have investigated the effects of MEA thickness and the concentrations of various species on the performance of the AA-DEFC working with the same anode and cathode like in the previous study. The authors found that the hydrogen peroxide concentration, ethanol concentration, NaOH concentration, and membrane thickness, have significant influences on the cell performance, reaching, with the MEA (a sodium conducting membrane based on pre-treated Nafion 211) possessing a thickness of 25 m, a PPD of 360 mW cm2.

Once again, in 2011, An et al.,273 only by exchanging the cathode (a bifunctional cathode: Au/NiCr foam with a loading of 1.2 mg cm2) of the cell improved the direct electro-reduction of H2O2 and lowered the H2O2 decomposition rate and the mass transport resistance; in this way the AA-DEFC yielded a PPD of 200 mW cm2 at 60 C.

In 2014, An et al.424 proposed an approach to avoid the mixed-potential phenomenon by introducing a redox couple of V(IV)/V(V) to the cathode while using hydrogen peroxide to chemically charge the redox ions, as illustrated in Fig. 22. Faster cathodic electrochemical kinetics was achieved even with no noble metal catalysts. This new alkalineacid DEFC yields a peak power density of 450 mW cm2 at 60 C.


Fig. 22 Schematic representation of the ethanol-hydrogen peroxide fuel cells. Reproduced from ref. 424, Copyright 2014 Royal Society of Chemistry.

A comparison of the above specified AA-DEFC performances is presented in Table 12.

Table 12 Comparison of alkalineacid DEFC performancesa
Anode Cathode MEA Temperature [C] PPD [mW cm2] Ref.
Catalyst Loading [mg cm2] Catalyst Loading [mg cm2]
a JM: Johnson Matthey catalyst.
PdNi/C 1 Pt/C (60 wt% Pt)-JM 3.9 CEM (Na+) (Nafion 117) 60 240 270
