5. Direct glycerol fuel cells (DGFCs)

5.1 Short overview

Glycerol is a surplus by-product obtained during the biodiesel manufacturing process by the transesterification of plant oils and animal fats and it accounts for 10% of the total product.425 Considered an ideal bio-renewable feedstock, the global glycerol market size was valued at 2.6 billion $ in 2019 and is expected to grow at a CAGR of 4.0% from 2020 to 2027.426 Moreover, the Oleoline Glycerine Market Report estimated that the price of crude glycerine will fall from $0.12 in June 2020 to $0.10 per pound by the end of 2021.427

Therefore, intensive research has been invested to develop processes based on glycerol, such as different catalytic methods to produce added value chemicals,428,429 but also processes to produce energy, for instance in fuel cell applications.321 In fact, glycerol was proposed for the first time in 1964 as a fuel for fuel cells430 due to its higher energy density (5965 W h L1) comparable with that of methanol (5897 W h L1) and close to that of ethanol (6307 W h L1), and demonstrated certain advantages such as low-fuel cross-over and high-power density.431,432 Since then, fuel cells directly fed with glycerol, named direct glycerol fuel cells (DGFCs) have recently gained significant interest and are a current focus in the fuel cell research community.433,434

DGFCs are classified into the following major types, schematically represented in Fig. 23: (i) alkaline type, using an anion exchange membrane (AEM-DGFCs) in which the OH anion is transported from the cathode to the anode; (ii) acid type, using a proton exchange membrane (PEM-DGFCs), in which the H+ is transported from the anode to the cathode, and (iii) alkalineacid type, which uses a cation exchange membrane (AA-DGFCs) and brings together the advantages of both alkaline and acid types.


Fig. 23 Schematic representation of the DGFC types: (a) alkaline direct glycerol fuel cell (AEM-DGFC), (b) acid direct glycerol fuel cell (PEM-DGFC) and (c) alkalineacid direct glycerol fuel cell (AA-DGFC).

Due to the fact that the electro-oxidation rate of glycerol in alkaline media is considerably higher than in acidic media, alkaline-direct glycerol fuel cells have gained more attention from the scientific community.435437

In this section the progress achieved over the last few years in the design of the electrocatalyst composition for all types of DGFCs will be reviewed, since they are vital components of fuel cell systems and represent a critical feature for the development of high-performance fuel cells.

For instance, there are studies438 that suggest gold and platinum based electrocatalysts as the best materials for glycerol oxidation in alkaline than in acid media, and Pt is less active than Au in alkaline medium (at high overpotentials).

Other studies demonstrated that Pt has the highest activity in the low potential region, with a turnover frequency (TOF) 45 times higher than that on Pd and 2030 times higher than that on Au at 0.3 V vs. Hg/HgO/1.0 M KOH.439

5.2 Alkaline direct glycerol fuel cells (AEM-DGFCs)

Glycerol electro-oxidation reaction is not a simple reaction because it involves the breakage of two CC bonds and therefore several pathways are possible, thus a deeper understanding of the whole process is highly desirable. For instance, in alkaline medium, during electro-oxidation of glycerol many soluble products are formed (see Scheme 9), such as glyceraldehyde, glyceric acid, glycolate, glycerate, glyoxylic acid, dihydroxyacetone, hydroxypyruvic acid, mesoxalic acid, oxalic acid, tartonate, oxalate and CO32 and several pathways are possible until it is completely oxidized to CO2 through the transfer of 14 electrons. The complete oxidation of glycerol to carbonate, in alkaline medium, is described by eqn (8):
C3H8O3 + 20OH 3CO32 + 14H2O + 14e(8)
Scheme 9 Products formed by glycerol oxidation on electrocatalysts in alkaline medium. Compounds in boxes have been detected in the anode compartments of DAFCs. The compound inside the circle has been exclusively detected in half cells. Reproduced with permission from ref. 321, Copyright 2009 American Chemical Society.

Re-adsorption and diffusion of the intermediary species have a negative impact on the total efficiency of the system. One way to improve the performance of the cell is to design an electrocatalyst440,441 and a support442,443 more resistant to the poisoning effect induced by the adsorbed intermediates and more selective towards the CO2 formation.440,444 There are only a few studies giving an overview of the advances in AEM-DGFC, in which different parameters affecting the cell performance are discussed,321,434,445 most of them presenting a summary on the electrocatalysts used in direct alcohol fuel cells.321,431,445

5.2.1 Noble metal based electrocatalysts for AEM-DGFCs. Pt based electrocatalysts have been identified as the most efficient electrocatalysts for AEM-DGFCs because they demonstrated high activity towards the electro-oxidation of alcohols in general,446448 and of glycerol in particular.437,449,450 Moreover, the higher the Pt percentage, the better the cell performance in the overall current density range, as a result of the enhanced kinetics of the glycerol oxidation and the enhanced mass transportation of the thinner catalytic layer, according to the data presented by Linares et al.450 Also, the fuel cell investigation demonstrated that the Pt/C (40 wt%) anode in AEM can yield a peak power density (PPD) of 124.5 mW cm2 with 2.0 M KOH + 1.0 M high purity glycerol at 80 C.437,439 This power current density is similar to the ones obtained in direct methanol (168 mW cm2) and ethanol (160 mW cm2) AEMFCs for multi-metallic catalyst systems (3 mgPtRu cm2 for methanol and 1 mgPd(NiZnP) cm2 for ethanol) under optimized experimental conditions (i.e. fuel composition flow rate, and temperature).374,451 It is important to note that Pt/C did not show a drop in performance when the fuel was switched to the biodiesel derived crude glycerol (88 wt%), and a PPD of 120 mW cm2 was obtained under similar conditions (2.0 M KOH + 1.0 M crude glycerol) indicating high stability of the Pt/C anode against the contamination/poisoning of the impurities in crude glycerol.439 However, the PPD presented a volcano-type behaviour as a function of crude glycerol concentration, and this can be explained by the fact that the lower glycerol concentration led to lower coverage rate of glycerol on the surface of the catalysts, while the higher crude glycerol will lead to a lower OH coverage on the catalyst surface, both situations result in a lower performance. The highest performance was obtained when the cell was fed with 6.0 M KOH + 1.0 M crude glycerol, the Pt/C anode exhibits a very high PPD of 184.2 mW cm2.439

However, Pt has several drawbacks and besides the poisoning problem, the price and its rare reserves are very important from the economic point of view and thus, developing efficient electrocatalysts free or containing low amounts of Pt is critical for the design of performant AEM-DGFCs. In this line, an unprecedented performance of about 269 mW cm2 was achieved by using the surface dealloyed PtCo supported on carbon nanotube (CNT) anode catalyst with only 0.5 mgPt cm2 in a membrane electrode assembly (MEA, with a commercial Fe-based cathode) for a direct crude glycerol (88 wt%)/O2 AEMFC operated at 80 C and ambient pressure.452 This performance is higher than the one obtained using Pt/CNTs (229 mW cm2), because of the d-band center of the Pt shift as a result of lattice strain and optimized electronic properties tuned by the sub-layer Co atoms, leading to weakened chemisorption of oxygenated species (see Fig. 24).


Fig. 24 Schematic illustration of the synthesis of surface dealloyed PtCo supported on CNT catalyst. Reproduced with permission from ref. 452, Copyright 2013 The Royal Society of Chemistry.

Recently, another transition metal used in combination in different proportions with Pt is copper. The tests made in fuel cells, directly fed with 2 M glycerol solution at 90 C, indicated PtCu/C (90:10) as the most effective electrocatalyst in the oxidation reaction of the fuels when compared with Pt/C and Cu/C, due to the synergy between the constituents of the electrocatalyst.453 The recorded PPD for PtCu/C (90:10) is about 18 mW cm2 while Pt/C and Cu/C showed 6 and 4 mW cm2, respectively.

Extensive research has been carried out to replace Pt, and compared to Pt-based electrocatalysts, less expensive and widely available Pd and/or Au based electrocatalysts have been exploited as substitutes in AEM-DGFCs due to their outstanding stability in alkaline media. Carbon supported Pd and Au nanoparticle (NP) catalysts with small diameters (3.5 nm), prepared through a solution-phase reduction method, showed at 80 C and 6.0 M KOH + 1.0 M crude glycerol a PPD of 93.9, and 50.1 mW cm2 on Pd/C (40 wt%), and Au/C (40 wt%) anodes, respectively.439 As in the case of Pt/C, the concentration of crude glycerol plays an important role, its effect on fuel performances is depicted in Fig. 25b and c. The PPD presented a volcano-type behaviour against the crude glycerol concentration. The highest PPDs were obtained with 3.0 M crude glycerol with Au/C anode catalysts, and 1.0 M crude glycerol with Pd/C catalyst.


Fig. 25 (a) Polarization and power density curves of Pt/C, Pd/C, and Au/C anode AEM-DGFCs with high purity glycerol fuel at 80 C; anode fuel: 2 M KOH + 1 M high purity glycerol; (b) effect of crude glycerol concentration on fuel cell performances using Pd/C and (c) Au/C anodes. Adapted with permission from ref. 439, Copyright 2013 Elsevier Publications.

From Fig. 25b and c we can observe that the crude glycerol fuel yielded lower open circuit voltages (OCVs) and PPDs on Pd/C and Au/C anode AEMFCs, as compared with Pt/C performances. Also, compared to the results obtained with high purity glycerol (in Fig. 25a), fed with 2.0 M KOH + 1.0 M crude glycerol, the OCVs on Pd/C and Au/C anode AEMFCs dropped by 0.08 and 0.16 V, respectively, while the PPD was reduced by 10.7 and 27.2 mW cm2, respectively. In the same study, the authors demonstrated that the anode electrode potential on Pd/C catalyst is governed by the catalyst surface coverage rate of both crude glycerol and OH.

If the crude glycerol concentration increases, it will drastically reduce the adsorption of OH and therefore a small increase in OCV is observed on the Pd/C anode. In the case of Au/C electrocatalyst, there are studies which indicated that the glycerol electro-oxidation will facilitate the formation of highly reactive glycerolate on the gold surface,454 which has, as a consequence, a higher OCV, and therefore, glycerol is expected to be a promising fuel for Au anode catalyst-based AEM-DGFCs.

Indeed, in a parallel study Li et al.454 obtained high performance using as the anode Au/C (30 wt% Au) electrocatalyst in an AEM-DGFC: at 80 C, the OCV and PPD reached 0.67 V and 57.9 mW cm2, respectively. However, in addition to the OH concentration and GLY concentration, the OH to GLY ratio seems to be as much as important for the high performance of DGFCs.455

Another study highlighted the excellent electrocatalytic activity of Pd supported on MWCNTs in terms of PPD, as high as 80 mW cm2 in a fuel cell working with glycerol at 80 C. The authors claim that such a remarkable electrocatalytic activity of Pd/MWCNTs can be associated both with the high dispersion of the metal particles and to the intrinsic properties of the MWCNTs.367

In the last few years, Li's group reported that the combination of two metals, Pd and Ag, with two different functions is more efficient than Pd for alcohol oxidation in general, and glycerol oxidation in particular.359 Thus, direct glycerol fuel cells achieved a PPD of 276.2 mW cm2, using PdAg/CNTs (0.5 mgPd per MEA1) with an average particle size of 2.7 nm, as an anode catalyst at 80 C and ambient pressure.359 This underlines the positive role of Ag being involved in the cleavage of the CC bond in the conversion of glycerol to oxalate, improving in this way the fuel efficiency of glycerol oxidation, while Pd can facilitate deprotonation of alcohol in a base electrolyte. In continuing this work, Pd, Ag mono, and bimetallic nanoparticles supported on carbon nanotubes (Ag/CNTs, Pd/CNTs, Pd1Ag1/CNTs, and Pd1Ag3/CNTs (20 wt%)) prepared using an aqueous-phase reduction method were used as working catalysts in an AEM-DGFCs.455 In this study, the PPDs are inferior to the one presented above, but also the cell parameters are different, such as different KOH concentrations (4 M vs. 6 M) or different reaction temperatures (60 C vs. 80 C). However, here also better results were obtained on the PdAg bimetallic catalyst due to the synergistic effect of promotion of intermediate aldehyde oxidation by Ag and CC breakage of C3 oxygenates along with a larger amount of Pd0 presented on the PdAg nanoparticle surface.

In the same line, last year, Pd and Ag were deposited on reduced graphene oxide (rGO) and it was shown that the interactions of PdAg NPs with rGO can control the performances through mediating the electronic characteristics of metal NPs and the reactant absorptions.456 With a peak current density of about 10328 A mgPd1 on Pd0.05Ag0.95/rGO it was possible to overcome the performance achieved by PdAg (1:1) deposited on CNTs (20% metal loading) of 8530 A mgPd1,359 the outstanding performance resulting from the intrinsic electron characteristics and lattice structure of PdxAgy(m)/rGO.456 Moreover, adding Ag to Pd, besides promoting the cell performance, also lowers the cost of the anode catalyst.

Since the Pd and Ag combination proved to be the most promising constituent for anodes in AEM-DGFCs, in order to further decrease the price of the fuel cell, parallel studies were conducted on PgAg/CNTs using polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE) thin films with different pore sizes as potential separators of fuel and oxidant streams.432

The performances of DGFCs with PTFE thin film were compared with an anion-exchange 116 membrane (A901) and the PPD of the DGFC with A901 AEM is 277.7 mW cm2 which is 22.6% higher than the PPD of the porous PTFE thin film. However, the authors claimed that using a PTFE thin film separator without further synthesis or modification breaks the paradigm and demonstrates that a PTFE thin film separator is capable of operating with lower ionic resistance than benchmark state-of-the-art anion exchange membranes.432

Literature survey indicates other binary or ternary palladium based electrocatalysts, since the addition of a second or a third element may modify the electronic or surface strain effects of catalyst surfaces. Thus, electrocatalysts such as PdAu/C (50:50), PdSn/C (50:50), PdAuSn/C (50:40:10) and PdAuSn/C (50:10:40)431 or Pd87Cu13/C,457 Pt3Sn/C,458 PdAg and PdAu nanoparticles loaded on carbon black,459 Pd(NiZn)/C460 or using different supports, such as Pd/Ti nanotube arrays were used as anodes in AEM-DGFCs,461 but with lower performances than the one presented and discussed previously in this review.

The effect of electrocatalyst nanostructure and morphology on the cell performances has also been the subject of several studies. For instance, in this year, strain engineering was studied, a new strategy that may open new avenues to highly efficient catalysts for direct alcohol fuel cells and many other applications.462 In this direction, the highly strained AuAgPd alloy nanowires outperformed the less strained counterparts, showing mass activities of up to 11.1 A mgPd1 for glycerol oxidation. The authors claimed that these activity values are an order of magnitude higher than those of the commercial Pd/C and higher than those of most Pd-based catalysts reported to date;463,464 suggesting promising applicability of these catalysts in DGFC applications.

Studies employing another interesting nanostructure were reported, for instance multimetallic Pd nanosponges with enhanced electrocatalytic activity for glycerol oxidation.465 The performances of PdAuNi nanosponge were due to the modified electronic states of Pd but also to the typical sponge nanostructures. The electrocatalytic activity was found to follow the order: Pd62Au21Ni17 > Pd64Au4Ni32 > Pd61Au31Ni8 >Pt/C. The peak current values of Pd62Au21Ni17 nanosponges reached 0.047 A cm2, which was twice as high as that of Pt/C (0.023 A cm2).

5.2.2 Noble metal-free electrocatalysts for AEM-DGFCs. Noble metal-free electrocatalysts for AEM-DGFCs as well as their oxides have attracted a lot of attention recently due to their low cost and abundance, yet their performances are below those of their counter-candidates based on noble metals. Thus, many electrocatalysts have been currently studied, especially based on Ni and Cu with good activity and high tolerance to CO poisoning.

For instance, a highly efficient electrocatalyst was developed using nickel nanoparticles supported on nitrogen doped graphene (Ni-NGr)466 that yielded a PPD of 10.4 mA cm2. This performance is higher than the one obtained on nickel ion implanted modified indium tin oxide (Ni-NP/ITO),467 and nickel nanoparticles stabilized by PVP.468 The authors indicated that the improved performance originated from the very high distribution of Ni nanoparticles on N-graphene which possessed a high surface area, but also to the presence of a synergistic combination of Ni nanoparticles and NGr sheets that induced a fast electron transfer effect due to nitrogen doped graphene.

In a recent study, the influence of the electrodeposited Ni nanoparticle morphology, distribution, structure and performance for energy production from glycerol was investigated.469 The support, commercial 3D-carbon fibers (CFs), was first functionalized with O- and S-like functionalities via electrochemical pretreatment in NaOH and H2SO4 and it was found that the crystallite size of the electrodeposited Ni is reduced from 154 nm for the untreated CF to 45 and 54 nm for the activated CF in H2SO4 and NaOH, respectively. The peak current of the glycerol oxidation exponentially increases with the electrodeposited Ni loading, while Ni modified pretreated CFs exhibited 2 fold higher electrocatalytic activity for glycerol electrooxidation at all the studied Ni loadings, suggesting the essential enhancing role of the preactivation step. In order to further enhance the performance of the glycerol electro-oxidation in alkaline medium, macroporous Ni electrodes decorated with Cu particles were reported.470 Indeed, the decoration with Cu was valuable and the electrocatalytic activity was enhanced as confirmed by the negative shift in the onset oxidation potential (ca. 100 mV) together with an increase in oxidation current that is up to 1.5-fold during the cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements, compared to the undecorated Ni foam.

Recently, Co3O4 has attracted great attention, being extensively investigated in electrochemical oxidation reactions due to its rich redox chemistry that results in high electrochemical activity.471,472 In glycerol oxidation, Co3O4 exhibited a current of 115 mV at 1.524 V using a 5 M KOH solution.

Copper oxides were employed as electrocatalysts based on the same arguments as for cobalt oxides, but their performance is seriously affected by the poor inherent conductivity of Cu2O.473 To overcome this issue, Cu nanoparticles were incorporated into Cu2O to enhance the conductivity and as a consequence the electrocatalytic activity of the Cu2O catalysts due to synergistic effects between Cu and Cu2O.474 Further doping with other metals (Ni, Fe, Co) of Cu/Cu2O dendrite foam structures induced high electrocatalytic activity and stability for glycerol electrooxidation.475

Although enormous effort has been devoted to the development of anodes containing non-noble metals for AEM-DGFCs in the last few years, the goal of achieving high performance is still unreachable at this moment.

5.3 Acid direct glycerol fuel cells (PEM-DGFCs)

Due to the smaller ohmic losses as compared to the alkaline electrolysers, the PEM electrolysers represent the best choice for the electrolytic production of hydrogen.476 The advantage of low ohmic losses makes the PEM electrolysers to operate at current densities greater than 1 A cm2, but require the use of mainly expensive and rare catalysts, such as platinum, ruthenium and iridium.

These appear to be the most stable and active electrode materials in acidic medium and represent the best option for performing the glycerol electro-oxidation process.477 Among them, platinum was considered unavoidable for a long time when talking about the activation and dissociative adsorption of glycerol at the electrode surface478 and the formation of adsorbed species that are further oxidized and leave behind protons. Thus, based on the use of platinum electrode materials, there are several papers dealing with the exploration of the processes arising from glycerol oxidation.444,479 Other mono-metallic480 or multi-metallic catalytic materials481484 can perform the electro-oxidation of glycerol.

Nevertheless, it should be stated that in the field of PEM-DGFCs most of the studies mainly report phenomena that are more related to the electrocatalytic glycerol oxidation and slightly or even not related to the real operation of the entire fuel cell ensemble.485,486 In the field of electrocatalytic glycerol oxidation, during the last decade, there were several excellent reviews477,487 focusing on describing the progress achieved in this research area.

5.4 Alkalineacid direct glycerol fuel cells (AA-DGFCs)

Improvement of DGFCs is required as the electrochemistry of these cells is challenging in several ways. As mentioned in the previous section, in AEM-DGFCs, unlike other simple alcohols, the electro-oxidation of glycerol is a very complex process.488 Finally, at the anode, the glycerol is only partially oxidized to CO2 and the process ended up to oxygenated products due to the difficulty to perform two CC bond cleavages and several parallel reactions. Another challenge is related to the reduction process taking place at the cathode. The Pt-specific PD of the most common Pt electrodes present values higher than 1.1 gPt kW1, while the targeted value is less than 0.2 gPt kW1. Such values can be achieved in theory at low Pt loadings (<0.05 mpPt cm2).489 However, lowering the Pt loadings was not sufficient to reduce the Pt-specific PD, due to the voltage loss. One of the most important contributions to voltage loss is the sluggish O2 reduction kinetics taking place at the cathode, which is a four-electron process with low probability of occurrence (eqn (9)).490
O2 + 4H+ + 4e 2H2O(9)

Besides all these aspects, the use of O2 as an oxidant implies the formation of water, which tends to flood the pores of gas diffusion layers and thus slowing down the reactant transport.270 To avoid these issues, hydrogen peroxide (H2O2) was found to be a good alternative as an oxidant to the cathode, with the following advantages: (i) the theoretical voltage of the fuel cell is considerably increased, from 1.23 V to 1.78 V at the cathode in DEFCs (see Fig. 15);270 (ii) H2O2 reduction process implies only the use of 2 electrons, thus offering a low activation barrier and therefore higher efficiency of the fuel cell (eqn (10)); (iii) can operate in an anaerobic environment, which might expand the use of DGFCs under underwater or outer space conditions491 and (iv) no water flooding problem as water is no longer a product derived from the reduction of H2O2.492 Moreover, H2O2 reduction in alkaline media has a theoretical voltage of 0.87 V, while under acidic conditions its reduction potential increased up to 1.78 V (see Fig. 15), making H2O2 a suitable oxidizer in acidic solutions.

H2O2 + 2e 2OH(10)

As presented in Fig. 15, combining the benefits of alkaline direct ethanol fuel cells with those of acid direct ethanol fuel cells (low activation of reduction reaction of H2O2 under acidic conditions), a new class with higher theoretical voltage values results (2.52 V). Therefore, extrapolating the data already existing for DEFCs, a new class of hybrid fuel cells using glycerol was born with the same goal of avoiding the drawbacks experienced with alkaline direct glycerol fuel cells and acid direct glycerol fuel cells. The expected theoretical voltage is 2.6 V.493

The structure of the hybrid fuel cell demands the use of: (i) an anode in an alkaline environment (usually sodium hydroxide) with a lower potential, (ii) a cationic exchange membrane (CEM) which allows the transfer of Na+ ions through the cathode, and (iii) a higher cathode potential in an acid environment (usually sulphuric acid). The schematic of such an alkalineacid fuel cell using H2O2 as the oxidant is presented in Fig. 23b.

Thus, the alkaline environment at the anode appears when sodium hydroxide (NaOH) is added to the glycerol solution (anolyte), so that hydroxyl ions are formed (see eqn (11)) which further migrate through the CEM to the cathode. At the same time, the glycerol oxidation reaction (GOR) will take place (see eqn (12)).

14NaOH 14Na+ + 14OH(11)
C3H8O3 + 14OH 3CO2 + 11H2O + 14e(12)

The acid environment at the cathode side, however, appears when sulphuric acid (H2SO4) is added and forms protons and ionized SO42 (see eqn (13)). The ionized SO42 species will further interact with the Na+ ions coming from the anode with the formation of sodium sulphate (Na2SO4) at the cathode side. In the meantime, the formed protons at the cathode and the electrons coming from the anode will interact with the oxidizer (H2O2) and water is formed in the catholyte (see eqn (14)).

7H2SO4 14H+ + 7SO42(13)
7H2O2 + 14H+ + 14e 14H2O(14)

Finally, the overall reaction of this alkalineacid direct glycerol fuel cell, with the theoretical voltage of 2.6 V, can be given by the following eqn (15):

C3H8O3 + 14NaOH + 7H2SO4 + 7H2O2 3CO2 + 25H2O + 7Na2SO4(15)

Taking into account that alkalineacid direct glycerol fuel cells possess all the advantages of a good anode and cathode working in alkaline and acid media, respectively, the challenge in using these hybrid fuel cells is provided by the working parameter conditions. Therefore, in the following we will discuss in detail the issues experienced in AA-DGFCs and the best operational parameters found for the alkalineacid direct glycerol fuel cells.

5.4.1 Operation conditions at the anode. Flow rate, glycerol concentration and variation of glycerol to sodium hydroxide ratio (G:NaOH) are usually evaluated to understand the influence of fuel flow and concentration. The flow rate of the fuel stream has very little impact on PD when the initial flow rate of 1 mL min1 was increased to 2 mL min1.494 This result was also confirmed by Sangkheaw et al.495 who found no significant difference in average current density for anolyte flow rates ranging from 1 mL min1 to 2 mL min1. However, decreasing the anolyte flow rate below 1 mL min1 will result in a significant drop of current density.

Regarding the concentration, at a high G:NaOH ratio of 2, a low power density (PD) of 50 mW cm2 was obtained, while a decrease of the ratio to 1 and further to 0.5 resulted in an increase of PD to 80 mW cm2 and 105 mW cm2, respectively.494 It can be concluded that the electrolyte concentration highly influences the overall fuel cell performance; for a constant NaOH concentration there is no reason to increase the glycerol concentration since this will determine a decrease of the PD. In this particular case, the pH ranges from 14.3 to 13.7 when the concentration of H2O2 increases. Such behaviour can be justified by the pH difference, which is directly related to the G:NaOH ratio. It was shown that a high pH value at the anode, when alcohols are used as the fuel, is very important for achieving a good cell performance.348

Table 13 presents the rate of oxidation of alcohols used in direct fuel cells and their dependency on the pH used. When the pH increases from 12 to 14 the oxidation rate increases by 12 orders of magnitude for each alcohol.

Table 13 The oxidation rate of each alcohol in the electrochemical cell reported after 5 min at 0.43 V vs. RHE in the pH range of 12 to 14 in 1 M fuel + appropriate concentration of [OH]348
Oxidation rate [mA cm2] at 0.43 vs. RHE
Alcohol pH
12 13 14
Ethanol 0.014 0.091 0.344
1-Propanol 0.009 0.073 0.390
2-Propanol 0.015 0.124 0.331
Ethylene glycol 0.002 0.017 0.117
Propylene glycol 0.03 0.043 0.172
Glycerol 0.001 0.011 0.064

Therefore, the oxidation rate of alcohols increases with the pH and is the main reason why direct alcohol fuel cells work well when a high concentration of NaOH is found in the fuel stream. However, Sangkheaw et al.,495 who also studied the influence of the G:NaOH ratio on the cell performance, noticed that a high concentration of NaOH results in an increase in viscosity, which consequently leads to mass transport issues at the cathode interface.

Thus, a compromise between a low G:NaOH ratio and increased viscosity must be found in order to obtain the optimum cell performance. Another alternative is to improve the microporous layer (MPL) at the anode so the mass transfer loss can be improved. Banjong et al.493 investigated the effect of MPL at the anode, using a hydrophilic and a hydrophobic MPL. This study suggests that the use of a hydrophobic MPL induces less fluctuation in the current density of the cell compared with the hydrophilic MPL or no MPL cells. The cell polarization indicates that the no-MPL cells outperform, however the presence of the hydrophobic MPL induces a satisfactorily high and stable current density of 432.57 mA cm2 with the standard deviation as low as 22.97 mA cm2.

5.4.2 Operation conditions at the cathode. One of the most important chemical reaction aspects that deserve great attention is H2O2 transformation, at high potentials, that can occur at the cathode. Hydrogen peroxide is unstable at high potentials and besides the reduction reaction, undesired reactions such as decomposition can occur with release of oxygen, protons and electrons (see eqn (16)):496
H2O2 O2 +2H+ + 2e(16)

Major issues can appear if the produced oxygen performs a competing reduction reaction with water formation (eqn (9)), which develops a much lower potential (1.23 V) than the H2O2 reduction reaction (1.78 V), leading to, as a consequence, a low voltage fuel cell.497

Besides the disadvantage of oxygen reduction at low potentials, the presence of O2 as a gas at the cathode interface can be an impediment in the H2O2 accessibility to the surface, resulting in a reduced performance of the AA-DGFC.493 Therefore, the variation of operating conditions at the cathode can be an alternative to avoid the oxygen formation/reduction at the interface.


Oxidant flowrate. Variation of hydrogen peroxide flowrate has been seen as a solution to avoid the formation of a higher amount of oxygen during the undesired decomposition of the oxidant. Banjong et al.493 observed a constant current density of 260 mA cm2 at 0.4 V, when applying 1, 3 and 6 mL min1 flow rates of H2O2 at the cathode (20 wt% Pt/C). However, intermittent drops along the discharge period were observed when a slow flowrate was used (1 mL min1) with a standard deviation of discharged current density of 40.33 mA cm2. Increasing the flow rates to 3 and 6 mL min1 diminished the standard deviation to 21.48 and 19.32 mA cm2 (see Fig. 26). Thus, a low flowrate was associated with a deficient transport of the oxidant to the cathode surface. Cell polarization also confirmed that higher flow rates increase the cell performance. The optimized flow rate was found to be 3 mL min1, since a lower flow rate resulted in unwanted drop of the current (as observed by chronoamperometry, Fig. 26), while at a higher flow rate, despite the high performance, the H2O2 utilization was too low.
Fig. 26 Chronoamperometry of the AA-DGFC at different oxidant flow rates. H2O2:H2SO4 ratio of 1, working temperature of 80 C, fuel: 1 M glycerol and 5 M NaOH. Reproduced with permission from ref. 493, Copyright 2020 Elsevier Publications.

Chino et al.494 observed an increase in power density when flow rates increased, when Pt/C and C black nanoparticles were used as cathodes. The working temperature of the cell was 60 C with 1 M glycerol as the fuel and 2 M NaOH. The increase of oxidant stream from 1 mL min1 to 2 mL min1 yielded a significant increase of power density from 68 mW cm2 to 105 mW cm2, when Pt-free C black nanoparticles were used. Using a Pt/C cathode, a much higher power density was observed for a 1 mL min1 flow rate (214 mW cm2), which increased to 253 mW cm2 for a 2 mL min1 flow rate. Even a lower power density was obtained when a Pt-free cathode was used, the results obtained are not negligible taking into account the cost consideration.


Oxidant concentration. Based on eqn (13) and (14), the H2O2:H2SO4 molar ratio should be 1.0, which was why Banjong et al.493 decided to keep the molar ratio constant at this value and varied the concentration of H2O2 (0.1, 0.3, 0.5 and 1.0 mol L1).

As can be observed in Fig. 27, the chronoamperograms show that the average current densities increase with the increase of H2O2 concentration as follows: for 0.1 mol L1 H2O2 67.15 mA cm2 current density was obtained, while an average current density of 239.67 mA cm2 was observed for 10 times higher H2O2 concentration. The cell polarization tests confirmed the results obtained by chronoamperometry. However, an abrupt drop in cell voltage was observed near 60 mA cm2 due to mass transfer limitation.


Fig. 27 Chronoamperometry of AA-DGFC at different H2O2:H2SO4 ratios and a fixed oxidant flow rate of 3 mL min1. Working temperature of 80 C, fuel: 1 M glycerol and 5 M NaOH. Reproduced with permission from ref. 493, Copyright 2020 Elsevier Publications.
5.4.3 Other parameters. AA-DGFCs were investigated to determine the best working temperature for this class of fuel cells. Chronoamperometry and cell polarization confirmed the same conclusion that increasing the temperature from 30 to 80 C will result in higher current density. Such behaviour can be rationalised by accepting that the high cell performance at high temperature is related to the high OCV, which is directly dependent on the temperature according to Nernst's equation.

The highest jump was observed when the temperature was increased from 70 to 80 C. Such a rise of current density can be explained by the glycerol electro-oxidation kinetics in alkaline medium, which is enhanced at higher temperature. Despite the remarkable current density observed, higher fluctuations in magnitude were registered at 80 C, therefore 70 C was chosen as the best working temperature for the AA-DGFC.

The Nafion ionomer content was found to increase the hydrophilicity of the anode when a hydrophilic MPL is used. Increasing the hydrophilicity will decrease the mass transport loss due to the high viscosity of the glycerolNaOH anolyte. An optimum content of 20% Nafion in MPL yielded a very good power density of the cell, 329 mW cm2. A higher content of Nafion will adsorb onto the MPL surface reducing its porosity, inhibiting the mass transport of the anolyte and deteriorating the cell performance.

6. New electrode configurations for portable hydrogen micro fuel cells

6.1 Short overview

Portable hydrogen fuel cells have received renewed attention in recent years driven by the progress made in fuel cells and hydrogen storage options.498 Compared with other portable fuel cells, their principal advantages are the easy anode reaction that allows for higher power densities per unit active area, and the cleaner combustion with sole production of water. On the other hand, they require stored hydrogen for operation which compromises the volume and weight energy densities. State-of-the-art hydrogen storage options for portable systems, like those based on metal-hydrides, complex compounds, and adsorbed hydrogen, practically attain around 10 gH2 kg1 and 100 gH2 dm3 storage densities that translate to effective power densities of 150 W h kg1 and 1500 W h dm3, depending on the storage media and assuming 50% conversion efficiency in the fuel cell. These values are similar to those of modern Li ion batteries.

According to the International Electrotechnical Vocabulary (IEV 485-09-23 (ref. 499)), a portable fuel cell is a fuel cell not intended to be permanently fastened or otherwise secured in a specific location. Conventional fuel cell designs of small size may fall in this category, without requiring any change in their configuration. A particular type of portable fuel cell is the micro fuel cell, wearable or easily carried by hand together with the fuel cell cartridge (IEV 485-09-21), which can produce electricity while ported in the hand of the user, like a battery. This type requires important changes in the cell design with respect to conventional fuel cells.

This section describes changes in the electrode configurations of a conventional PEMFC to make a portable micro fuel cell. PEMFC is most convenient for hydrogen portable applications due to the fast power onoff, low working temperature, and high-power density. The first part describes essential differences in the configuration and operation of a portable PEMFC with respect to a conventional PEMFC. In the second part, some main features of anodes and cathodes for portable PEMFCs are described.

6.2 Convective and passive PEMFC configurations

Conventional PEMFCs require a convective flow of the reactant gases through the electrodes, consisting of hydrogen and air streams flowing at the back of the anode and cathode, respectively, parallel to the cell plane. The main purpose is to accelerate the transport of reactants towards the catalyst layers and the rejection of the water produced, with other positive effects such as the homogenization of reaction rates and temperature over the active area region and helping the rejection of excess heat from the cell. Gas convection is essential to attain power densities above 1 W cm2 with respect to the active area in conventional cells.

However, convective operation requires elements that increase the weight, volume, and cost of the stack and fuel cell system, which make it unsuitable for small portable power. The flow of gases is set by grooved plates at the back of the porous electrodes, called flow-field or bipolar plates, which also serve as current collectors and cell separators in a stack. Such plates, preferentially metal or graphite plates, contribute significantly to the weight (>70%) and volume (>80%) of the stack, and about 15% of the cost.500 In addition, auxiliary elements are necessary to maintain the gas flow, like fans, valves, purging and recirculation systems, gas conditioning, and stack thermal control, which further increase the weight, volume, and cost of the fuel cell system, while decreasing the power efficiency due to auxiliary consumption. The auxiliary charges and efficiency losses of the convective PEMFC configuration make it unsuitable when scaling to small portable applications, where the dimensions, weight, autonomy, safety, and operability requirements are different than in larger power systems.498

In the passive PEMFC configuration the fuel cell can generate a constant power without applying convective forces to gases. The anode and cathode operate in contact with static hydrogen and air atmospheres, which avoid the use of convective components and favour fuel cell system portability. The transport of gases and water occurs by passive processes, like capillary diffusion and natural convection caused by gradients in chemical potentials, density, and temperature within the electrodes and around the cell.501503 Passive conditions require optimising liquid water interaction and transport within the porous electrodes, to sustain continuous current generation. Fig. 28 compares the schemes of a convective and a passive fuel cell.


Fig. 28 Working principles of (a) the conventional-convective, and (b) the full-passive PEMFC configurations.

The following parts of Section 6 describe changes implemented in the architecture of the anode and cathode to attain maximal power output in passive operation.

6.3 The passive dead-end anode

Operating in a static atmosphere of hydrogen, without forcing hydrogen flow or purging,504,505 allows for more simple auxiliary systems and the possibility to attain 100% faradaic efficiency, which benefit the portability of the fuel cell system. On the other hand, passive transport of hydrogen and heat requires changes in the anode electrode design to maintain high power production rates. The principal transport limitation in a passive anode is caused by the accumulation of water in the pores, coming from the cathode, which blocks catalyst centres and leads to cell failure.501,505 To avoid anode flooding, some changes in the conventional anode design must be introduced, like those described below. Of course, dry hydrogen feeding will be most convenient for a passive dead-end anode, like that obtained from a metal-hydride canister or from compressed hydrogen storage.
6.3.1 Water permeable window. Adding a water permeable window to the anodic chamber for water elimination from the cell was first reported by Karst et al.,506 and incorporated by some of us in functional portable fuel cells.504 The anodic window must be made of a hydrophilic membrane able to absorb and transport water driven by a chemical potential gradient in liquid water and/or vapour (humidity gradient). At the same time, the membrane must be impermeable to hydrogen gas, and have appropriate mechanical strength and chemical stability.

Materials for water permeation membranes are preferentially based on modified polymer matrices with hydrophilic components. Nafion polymer has been used as an anodic PEMFC window,504 whose structure, combining a hydrophobic fluorocarbon backbone and hydrophilic side polar chains, favours channel formation upon swelling for liquid water conduction with a high self-diffusivity of 106 to 105 cm2 s1.507 One drawback of this material is the high interfacial transport resistance across the vapourNafion interface that hinders water vapour permeation, whereas it is negligible for the liquidmembrane interface.507,508 Consequently, anode flooding could occur in the cell before the window starts to conduct water. The use of other materials for anodic water permeation in a PEMFC is a matter of future research, such as those based on hydrophilic polymers like poly(ethylene oxide), PEO-copolymers, or hydrophobic polymers blended with hydrophilic materials.

The water permeable window must be suitably placed in the anodic chamber of the cell, for easy access of water, and probably assisted with hydrophilic paths to accelerate water movement. A cross-sectional scheme of a cell with a permeable water window in the anode chamber is shown in Fig. 29.


Fig. 29 Cross-sectional scheme of a passive PEMFC cell with a passive dead-end anode with a water permeable window, and air-breathing cathode. Reproduced with permission from ref. 505, Copyright 2020 Elsevier Publications.

The permeable water window has some drawbacks. It requires the anode back plane in contact with the ambient environment so it may be appropriate only for planar stacking configurations. In addition, it increases the volume and cost of the cell, so it may not be suitable for large serial stacking.

6.3.2 Hydrophobic anode. Another means to prevent dead-end anode flooding in a passive dead-end anode is by setting a hydrophobic catalyst layer that impedes cathodic water passing to the anode gas diffusion layer. A previous study has shown that using hydrophobic catalyst layers changes liquid water transport inside a PEMFC.509 For instance, a superhydrophobic cathode forces liquid water towards the anode in a conventional PEMFC and facilitates a high hydration level in the membrane which is convenient for the convective operation. In the passive PEMFC, a superhydrophobic anode may be more advantageous to prevent water back-diffusion from the cathode, since lower current densities are attained and membrane drying out is less probable due to the high-water content attained in the cathode (see below).

Hydrophobicity in the catalyst layer is achieved by adding a hydrophobic agent like PTFE.510 This procedure is used for the hydrophobization of gas diffusion layers to improve their liquid water transport. Its use in catalyst layers is also reported, though it poses problems due to the blocking of pores and Pt particles that may lead to a decrease in porosity, catalyst active area, and electronic conductivity of the layer. Such deleterious effects may be prevented by adding carbon nanotubes,511 but it increases the complexity of the catalyst layer preparation and operation.

The second way to confer hydrophobic character to the catalyst layer without the need for additives, is by depositing the layer using the electrospray technique, which confers a dendritic morphology with hydrophobic properties (Fig. 30).512,513 Electrospray deposition of catalytic suspensions of the Pt/C particles and the ionomer (Nafion) has proven to be useful for the hydrophobization of the cathodic catalyst layer of conventional cells.509


Fig. 30 SEM images of the morphology of a Pt/C catalyst layer prepared by the electrospray method, showing the surface (left) and cross-section with open pore walls (right). Inset: water dropsurface interaction according to the CassieBaxter model.

6.4 The air-breathing cathode

In the passive air-breathing cathode, oxygen, water, and heat are exchanged with the ambient environment by natural forces. Among other properties, the passive cathode works under high saturation conditions in the catalyst and gas diffusion layers,505 with consequent increase in the tortuosity of the oxygen diffusion path which decreases the maximum current generation. At exceedingly high saturation levels, the access of oxygen to the catalyst particles may be available from the dissolved phase, even further limiting the current. To palliate the severe limitations posed by passive operation, conditions for liquid water interaction and passive transport within the pores must be optimised, involving the porous layers of the cathode and the cathodic plate design. Passive water transport also makes the cell response highly dependent on the ambient environment parameters, like humidity, temperature, natural convection, and cell orientation.
6.4.1 Limiting transport. The operation of a passive air-breathing cathode is mostly limited by the high liquid water content (saturation), which depends on the porosity of the catalyst and gas diffusion layers and on the hydrophobicity of pore walls. Saturation conditions give rise to two transport regimes for liquid water.514 Under high saturation values, above the irreducible value (s > sw0), funicular saturation connects both faces of the electrode with liquid water domains which activates the capillary transport driven by a difference in water pressure between the cathodemembrane interface and the ambient environment. Below the irreducible saturation, the absence of liquid paths allows water transport by slow evaporationcondensation events between the insular liquid domains. Since passive cathodes normally operate under high saturation, the evacuation of water by capillary transport is active in the steady-state, and the limiting current density (iL,H2O) can be related to a liquid water pressure difference by means of Darcy's law:
(17)
where F (= 96485 C mol1) is the Faraday constant, w (= 1 kg m3) the water density, Mw (= 18 g mol1) the water molar weight, kw (m2) the intrinsic water permeability, w (Pa s) the viscosity of water, pw (Pa) the water phase pressure, and L the electrode gas diffusion layer thickness (for simplification liquid water transport limitation will be entirely attributed to water content of this layer which is reasonable because it is normally much thicker than the catalyst layer). kw depends on water saturation (kw = k(sw sw0)/(1 sw0)m, where k is the permeability of the porous phase, sw0 the irreducible water saturation, and m = 4.5 for the gas diffusion layer of fuel cell electrodes). The water pressure gradient is related to the water saturation gradient in the porous electrode by the capillary pressure (pc):
(18)
where pg (Pa) is the gas phase pressure, (N m1) the surface tension, i the internal contact angle, the layer porosity, and J (s) the Leverett function.509 The gas pressure (pg) is considered constant in the porous media and equal to the ambient air pressure. Saturation of the porous layers also limits oxygen access to the catalyst layer, leading to a limiting current given by the equation:
(19)
where c* is the oxygen concentration in the ambient environment and Deff is the effective diffusivity of the porous layer, which for a gas diffusion layer is given by:515
Deff = Db3.5(1 s)2.15(20)
where Db is the open space, outside the porous layer, diffusivity.

Eqn (17) and (19) describe the two possible current limitations by the gas diffusion layer, by oxygen and by water, as a function of water saturation. Both expressions are compared in Fig. 31 for values of standard PEMFC electrodes, showing that limiting conditions change from water transport to oxygen transport at about s = 0.5, where a maximum limiting current can be attained under passive conditions of 1.5 A cm2. Experimental values of limiting currents normally fall below 0.5 A cm2, reflecting the passive air-breathing cathode operating under oxygen transport limitation due to high pore saturation, s > 0.7. The optimal saturation condition is around s = 0.40.6, which improves oxygen diffusivity while maintaining sufficient water content for electrochemical reactions and capillary transport. Such values require the preparation of porous layers with an optimised porous structure and hydrophobicity, applying to the cathode the same methods described in 6.3.2 for anode hydrophobization.


Fig. 31 Limiting currents at a passive air-breathing cathode as a function of water saturation, according to the expressions in eqn (17) and (19). Parameter values: k = 3 108 cm2, w = 103 Pa s, L = 400 m, = 72 105 N cm1, i = 150, = 0.8, c* = 8.5 105 mol cm3, Db = 0.20 cm2 s1, T = 295 K, p = 101325 Pa, RH = 0.3, J (s) as in ref. 516.
6.4.2 The cathodic plate. The cathodic plate of a passive air breathing cell provides compression by clamping pressure to decrease contact resistance between the electrode and the current collector. At the same time, it allows the exchange of air, water, and heat with the ambient environment. Many designs are proposed based on planar rigid plates perforated with holes, as shown in Fig. 32a, where parameters like hole shape and size, open area ratio, plate material, and thickness, must be optimized to improve mass transport and heat transport, and decrease the contact resistance.517 Cell water removal and emission from the plate to the ambient environment must be optimised via natural forces (evaporation, natural convection, or gravity).
Fig. 32 Images of passive air breathing cells with (a) cathodic planar, and (b) columnar plates. Reproduced with permission from ref. 518 and 505, respectively, Copyright 2009 and 2020, respectively, Elsevier Publications.

A columnar plate has recently been used to maximize water removal rate and air-breathing performance (see Fig. 32b).505 It allows for a higher mobility of water droplets over the cathode surface which facilitates their removal. The columnar plate makes the cell response dependent on orientation, as shown in Fig. 33.


Fig. 33 Comparing fuel cell performances with a columnar passive air-breathing cathode in three orientations. Reproduced with permission from ref. 505, Copyright 2020 Elsevier Publications.

The cathodic plate also has a significant effect on heat dissipation from the air-breathing cell. Heat generated by entropic and Joule heating may make the temperature of the cell too high for PEMFC operation. Dissipation by natural forces depends on the cell material, plate design, ambient conditions, and orientation. A reduced heat transfer occurs with the cathode facing downwards,519 and may limit cell response by membrane drying.520 Passive air breathing must operate in a delicate equilibrium between membrane drying and electrode flooding which rely on the optimal design of the cell and packaging.521

6.5 Summary

Characteristics of electrodes and cell designs are described for the adaptation of a hydrogen PEMFC to a portable micro fuel cell. The portability is favoured by changing from a convective flow of reactants to passive feeding. Passive feeding poses significant limitations that require making necessary changes in electrode and cell configurations to allow for a continuous operation with maximal power delivery. The passive anode should work under full dead-end conditions, without purging, to attain 100% faradaic efficiency. Avoiding the flooding of the anode is essential for a continuous operation. The passive air-breathing cathode requires optimising the action of natural forces for the exchange of water and oxygen with the ambient environment. Gross power attainable with a passive PEMFC is typically lower than that with a conventional-convective PEMFC, but the overall net system power and power densities are adequate for portable applications with similar performance to modern batteries.

7. Concluding remarks

In the group of the emerging, clean energy technologies comprising the solar energy, geothermal energy, wind energy, and fusion power technology, fuel cells are in a pole position with regard to the effective and practical way of conversion of hydrogen into electricity.

This review article describes and discusses the state-of-the-art of the electrocatalysts used in the last few years in different fuel cells. It is important to note that the relationship between physico-chemical properties and cell performances is decisive for the design of efficient electrocatalysts.

Various catalysts with unique structures and morphologies, such as nanowires, nanosheets, and assembly structures, have been manufactured in recent years, and some of them show excellent catalytic performance characteristics. It is evident that the electrocatalysts must meet several stringent criteria to achieve reliable, high and constant performances, such as (1) high porosity adequate for alcohol and oxygen transport, (2) sufficient electronic conductivity; (3) chemical stability and high C and CO tolerance (assured by well-defined chemical states, which do not suffer major changes) in a reducing fuel atmosphere; (4) compatibility with other DAFC components; (5) ease of fabrication; (6) relatively low cost induced by avoiding the use of Pt or using an ultra-low Pt amount.

Improving the portability of hydrogen PEMFC and DAFC may require new electrode configurations as described in Section 6. In particular, the structures facilitating the passive operation of the anode and cathode are most convenient to alleviate weight and volume, and to increase the net power production of the systems by avoiding auxiliary components required in the conventional-convective fuel cells.

The results discussed in this paper show that fuel cells are promising power sources for portable applications for the near future. Most appropriate are low temperature fuel cell types fed with hydrogen (Section 2), methanol (Section 3), ethanol (Section 4), or glycerol (Section 5). Hydrogen fuel cells provide high power density (>1000 mW cm2) in conventional cell configurations, but a decrease is expected in real portable operation, under passive feeding conditions. These cells rely on portable hydrogen storage options which limit the energy density. Liquid fuels allow for higher energy density, and promising power densities have been attained in laboratory cells, above 200 mW cm2 for methanol, and 100 mW cm2 for ethanol and glycerol. Future improvements should concentrate on optimized electrode and fuel cell designs for reliable and high performance operation under portable power conditions.
